8 June 2005
The Wall Street Journal

GM SAID it plans to cut about 25,000 North American jobs, or 14% of the region's work force, by 2008. CEO
Rick Wagoner said the auto maker also will close more plants and is studying "strategic options" for its
financing arm. Meanwhile, Kerkorian's tender offer for 28 million GM shares was set to expire at 5 p.m.
yesterday.
---
Northrop and Airbus's parent agreed in principle to join forces in a bid to break Boeing's monopoly on
supplying aerial-refueling planes to the Pentagon.
Airbus parent EADS made a conditional offer to give up aircraft loans from European nations in a bid to revive
U.S.-EU talks.
---
Sears posted a $9 million loss for the first quarter to include Kmart, hurt by a $90 million charge for an
accounting change.
---
Manufacturing growth is slowing due to high energy prices and a scramble to trim inventories, with Western
Europe hardest hit.
---
China's central-bank chief said pressure on Beijing to revalue its currency is making it more difficult for China
to take action.
---
The Dow industrials ended up just 16.04 at 10483.07 after remarks that the Fed isn't done raising rates
erased most earlier gains.
---
CEOs of Delta and Northwest warned Chapter 11 filings are likely if Congress doesn't make it easier to fund
pension plans.
---
Unicredito Italiano's purchase of HVB for $19.6 billion in stock is expected to be approved by both banks'
boards this weekend.
---
Third Avenue proposed buying Instinet's brokerage unit for $307 million, a move that could delay Nasdaq's
electronic-trading deal.
---
Bank regulators are growing more worried about credit risk amid an easing of underwriting standards, a Fed
governor warned.

China Shenhua Energy's IPO likely will be priced today in Hong Kong at the lower end of the proposed price

---
AOL will unveil a revamped Web site this month, offering free many features once available only to paying
subscribers.
---
Texas Instruments raised its outlook for second-quarter profit and sales, citing improving demand for its chips
and calculators.
---
LG.Philips signed a three-year deal, valued at about $5 billion, to supply H-P with flat-panel screens.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: May Markets Confound Investors --- Strong Advances in Energy, Technology...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: May Markets Confound Investors
--- Strong Advances in Energy, Technology Defy Forecasts; A Rough Month for Asia
By Peter A. McKay
637 words
6 June 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WHETHER GLOBAL investors made money in the past month turned on whether they bought into a couple
of widely held assumptions.
One was the prediction going into May that energy demand would tail off. The other was that interest rates
would rise and crimp spending, especially on technology.
Those forecasts didn't pan out, of course. Crude futures rallied more than $2 a barrel in May and have nearly
doubled that gain already in June. In all, crude has rallied about 5% since the start of May to trade at more
than $55 on the New York Mercantile Exchange. It isn't seen weakening now that the post-Memorial Day
driving season has begun in the U.S. and data are showing weak inventories of refined fuels like heating oil
and diesel.
Analysts are talking about the return of oil-related economic fears that not too long ago seemed to be waning.
"People had written the death notice for oil, but now that mentality is changing back," says energy analyst Phil
Flynn of Alaron Trading Corp. "We're back to a scenario where people are thinking world-wide consumption
could outstrip production."
In June's early going, other factors are increasing investors' jitters about the U.S. economy, including Friday's
lackluster jobs report. Even one of Wall Street's longtime beacons of hope -- rising Chinese demand for
resources and finished goods -- is looking iffy.
And yet, some industries are still seeing nice cash flow, namely technology. In particular, demand for laptop
computers has remained unexpectedly strong, says Tobias Levkovich, chief U.S. equity strategist at
Citigroup's Smith Barney unit.
Tech's resilience is evident in the performance of the Nasdaq Composite Index and other tech-heavy
indicators. Since the start of May, the composite is up 7.8%, or 149.78 points, to 2071.43, while the Dow
Jones technology indexes tracking the sector in the U.S., Europe and globally are all up more than 6% over
the same period.
Mr. Levkovich adds that much of the buying in tech stocks was likely by bearish investors covering downbeat
"short" positions left over from their aggressive push to get out of the sector in April. "There's an interesting
split of opinion on Wall Street right now regarding tech," says Mr. Levkovich. "I don't necessarily think a lot of
the recent buyers are a convinced bunch."
Elsewhere, several indicators tied closely to Asia have fared poorly lately, hurt by expectations of an
economic slowdown in China. The benchmark Shanghai Composite Index has plummeted since the start of
May, off 13%, or 145.5 points, to 1013.637. In Japan, the Nikkei 225 eked out a 2.6% gain, although eight
other indexes covering various sectors of that country's market were in the red.
The dollar has risen against overseas currencies, hurting gold, which is often viewed as an alternative means
of financial exchange at times when people lose confidence in paper money. Since May 1, gold futures are
down $10.30, or 2.4%, to $425.80 a troy ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
Despite the metal's recent struggles as a financial asset, physical demand for gold to make jewelry and other
items has remained strong.
"If you look at the stronger dollar and other things working against gold, it's really hung in there in terms of
price," says George Milling-Stanley, manager of investment and market intelligence at the World Gold
Council, a mining-industry group.

--- Page 12 ---
Page 12 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The J.P. Morgan trade-weighted dollar index is up 1.7%, or 1.5 point, to 90.8 since May 1.

Business and Finance
Business and Finance
What's News
Business and Finance
528 words
6 June 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
APPLE IS EXPECTED to start shifting its Macintosh line next year to Intel chips, in a major change of
strategy for the computer maker. The move could be a blow to IBM and Freescale, which now supply Apple's
PowerPC chips. But it could help Apple remain competitive with rivals like Dell and be a prelude to
collaboration with Intel in developing new devices for homes and offices.
---
Bristol-Myers is set to pay about $300 million and name director James Robinson chairman to settle a
criminal probe of alleged accounting manipulations.
---
ProLogis is buying Catellus in a $3.6 billion deal that will form the largest REIT focused on warehouse and
distribution properties.
---
Agilent put its semiconductor division on the auction block, in a retreat from a business that has been a drain
on profitability.
---
E*Trade sweetened its bid for Ameritrade, hoping to derail the rival online brokerage firm's discussions with

Deal Targets and Airlines Serve as Driving Forces
Small-Stock Focus
Deal Targets and Airlines Serve as Driving Forces
By Mohammed Hadi Dow Jones Newswires
616 words
3 June 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Small stocks built on Wednesday's gains, in a relatively quiet session ahead of the monthly
nonfarm payroll report due early Friday.
Airlines were strong, as they began to report traffic for May. Shares of Continental Airlines -- whose traffic
rose 10.8% -- added $1.10, or 8.1%, to $14.67 on the New York Stock Exchange. Delta Air Lines gained 36
cents, or 9.4%, to 4.18, also on the NYSE, and Northwest Airlines gained 47 cents, or 8%, to 6.38.
Mergers and acquisitions drove some gains. Notable among small caps was the purchase of Shopping.com,
an online shopping-comparison site, by eBay for $620 million, or $21 a share. Shopping.com gained 3.33, or
19.1%, to 20.77. Lexington, Mass., based Stride Rite said it will buy rival shoe company Saucony for $170
million, about $23 a share. Saucony's class A shares rose 3.30, or 17.1%, to 22.65, and Stride Rite's rose
1.46, or 12.1%, to 13.50.
Scott Jacobson, senior trader at Jefferies &Co., said the stock market was likely waiting to judge the effect of
this morning's payroll report on interest rates. "Other than earnings, interest rates are the primary driver of
valuation in the stock market," he explained, "and of course the bonds aren't doing anything, so stocks aren't
doing anything."
The Russell 2000 Index rose 1.50, or 0.2%, to 625.24, the S&P SmallCap 600 Index rose 0.91, or 0.3%, to
327.41, and the Nasdaq Composite Index of large and small stocks rose 9.94, or 0.5%, to 2097.80.
The dollar pulled back a bit, boosting gold and gold-mining stocks. Bema Gold added 9 cents, or 4.2%, to
2.25, on the American Stock Exchange, and Lihir Gold's American depositary shares rose 79 cents, or 4.8%,
to 17.40.
Retailers were active Thursday after a number reported sales for May. Fashion retailer Wet Seal, of Foothill
Ranch, Calif., added 70 cents, or 16.7%, to 4.90. It said same-store sales in May rose 56.9%. Topeka,
Kan.-based shoe retailer Payless ShoeSource added 92 cents, or 5.4%, to 17.99 after same-store sales rose
4.1%, topping analysts' expectations. Pier 1 Imports, by contrast, fell 89 cents, or 5.4%, to 15.74 on the
NYSE. The Fort Worth, Texas, home-furnishings chain posted a worse-than-expected 7.7% drop in
same-store sales, and cut first-quarter guidance.
Cantel Medical plunged 12.79, or 41%, to 18.16, making it one of the Big Board's worst performers. The Little
Falls, N.J., provider of infection prevention and control products lost a major distribution agreement. The
company also said its third-quarter earnings rose 23%.
Anadys Pharmaceuticals, of San Diego, rose 1.61, or 23.8%, to 8.37. It signed a global agreement with
large-cap Novartis to produce and commercialize Anadys's Hepatitis C treatment, ANA975.
Carmike Cinemas fell 1.47, or 4.4%, to 31.88. Bear Stearns cut its rating to underperform from peer perform,

Oxford Industries (NYSE) added 4.09, or 11.3%, to 40.38. The Atlanta private-label clothier said it expects
earnings for the next two quarters to beat the average analyst estimate.

Stocks Advance On Rate Hopes, Oil-Price Drop
By E.S. Browning
481 words
3 June 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
OPTIMISM ABOUT interest-rate prospects combined with a decline in oil prices to help stock indexes scratch
out more gains, sending them once again to their highest levels in more than two months.
Treasury-bond yields, whose recent decline has helped to spark the stock rally, were little changed, leaving
the yield of the 10-year Treasury note near the 14-month low hit Wednesday, still well below 4%.
Continuing hopes that the Federal Reserve will stop raising its target short-term rates this summer, and a
late-day dip in oil prices, helped stocks rebound from early profit-taking. The Dow Jones Industrial Average
edged up 3.62 points, or 0.03%, to 10553.49, its highest finish since March 21. It still is down 2% for 2005.
"The biggest underlying theme seems to be this creeping notion that we are very close to the end of the
tightening cycle for the Fed," said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at San Francisco brokerage firm Wells
Fargo Securities.
Investors shrugged off news that labor costs, a much-watched indicator of inflation, rose at the fastest rate in
four years during the first quarter. But money managers will be closely watching the May job-creation
numbers, due today, for hints about whether inflation remains tame. Trying to prevent inflation is the main
reason the Fed raises rates.
Oil futures briefly moved above $55 a barrel on expectations of heavy summer demand, but then fell to
$53.63 a barrel, down 97 cents, on the New York Mercantile Exchange, after a government report showed
inventories rising.
The dollar, on a tear against the euro after France rejected the proposed European constitution, finally fell
back slightly. Gold, which traders use to bet against the dollar and which has been weak this year, staged its
biggest daily gain in 10 months, rising $7.40, or 1.8%, to $422.70 an ounce.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.17%, or 2.02 points, to 1204.29, down just 0.6% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.48%, or 9.94, to 2097.80, off 3.6% for 2005. The S&P is at its highest since
March and the Nasdaq at its highest since January.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.24 billion shares traded, 1,978 stocks rose and
1,329 fell.
Bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note fell 1/32, or 31.25 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield up to 3.896%. The 30-year bond rose 6/32 to yield 4.229%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 108.26 yen, down from 108.75; the euro rose to $1.2277
from $1.2203.

Treasurys and Dollar Rally on French Vote, But Blue Chips Slide
Tuesday's Markets
Treasurys and Dollar Rally on French Vote, But Blue Chips Slide
By E.S. Browning
817 words
1 June 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TREASURY BONDS and the dollar soared in the first day of U.S. trading following France's rejection of
Europe's proposed constitution.
But fresh signs of uncertainty in the U.S. economy, which were taken as good news for bonds, helped knock
stocks down.
Surging Treasury bonds pushed the yield of the benchmark 10-year Treasury note down below to the
much-watched 4.0% level in late New York trading, its lowest since February. In subsequent trading, it fell
below 3.99%, a level rarely seen.
Worries about European cooperation, meanwhile, helped push the euro to $1.2305, its lowest level against
the dollar in almost eight months in late New York trading. The dollar hit a two-month high against the yen of
108.53. France voted on Sunday but U.S. markets were closed Monday in observance of the Memorial Day
holiday.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which rose 70.64 points last week, retreated 75.07 points, or 0.71%,
yesterday to 10467.48. It finished with a gain of 2.7% for the month of May, but still is down 2.9% for 2005.
May was its best month this year, ending a two-month losing streak.
Stocks had risen sharply in the previous two weeks and were due for a pullback. Some investors also were
nervous because of a report that manufacturing growth in the Chicago region slowed sharply in May.
The day's big action was in the currency and bond markets, where investors were dumping euros and
speculating about how many more times the Federal Reserve will raise its short-term interest-rate targets
before calling a halt.
The dollar's strength -- it rose 4.6% in May against the euro, its third consecutive monthly advance -- reflects
signs of economic weakness in Europe as well as the political disarray shown in the French vote.
The flight to dollars helped Treasurys because investors often use Treasury bonds as a vehicle for
dollar-buying. That could help explain the past few weeks' strength in bond prices, and the decline in bond
yields.
In addition, more economists have been pointing to softening commodity prices as a sign that economic
activity is slowing world-wide, including in China, which would hold down inflation. That, in turn, would
reduce the need for the Fed to keep raising U.S. interest rates. And slow growth, with low inflation, makes
Treasury bonds a more attractive investment.
"There is a migration toward the idea that the economy is really slowing down," said Henry Herrmann, chief
investment officer at mutual-fund group Waddell &Reed in Overland Park, Kan.
Investors sometimes use the 4% level for the 10-year yield as a bogey -- selling bonds and taking profits
when yields fall that low, which in turn tends to drive yields back up and prices down. The last time the
10-year yield finished below 4%, in February, it stayed there for one day.
To see whether yields can remain this low, and whether stocks can rebound, investors will be watching a
report today on national manufacturing activity. On Friday comes the Labor Department report on May job
creation, another much-awaited sign of economic strength.
One positive economic sign yesterday was the Conference Board's report that its consumer-confidence
survey unexpectedly rose sharply in May, but it was largely ignored.

--- Page 20 ---
Page 20 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 7.6% in May, its strongest month since October 2003, despite yesterday's
decline of 0.36%, or 7.51 points, to 2068.22. It remains down 4.9% this year.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 3% in the month, its best month this year. It fell 0.61%, or 7.28
points, yesterday to 1191.50. It is off 1.7% for 2005.
Gold fell $3.50 to $416.30 an ounce, a decline for May of $18.70, or 4.3%, its third-largest monthly decline in
the past year. Gold often falls when the dollar rises, since traders often play the two against each other.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.66%, or 1.12 points, to 167.37.

Major stock indexes retreated. But on the Big Board, where 1.48 billion shares traded, 1,683 stocks rose and

Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 20/32, or $6.25 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
down to 4.000%. The 30-year bond was up 1 19/32 to yield 4.333%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 108.53 yen, up from 107.97, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2305 from $1.2472.

Mr. Greenspan's Cappuccino
Mr. Greenspan's Cappuccino
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,277 words
31 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Get out your dictionary and thesaurus: Alan Greenspan is at it again. In 1996, he suggested that stock
investors suffered from "irrational exuberance," a few months ago he labeled surprisingly low long-term
interest rates a "conundrum" and this month he said that there was "a little froth" in the housing market,
calling it an "unsustainable underlying pattern."
In the dictionary, "froth" is described as a mass of bubbles, or foam. It brings to mind a cappuccino, not a
realtor's office. Nonetheless, it is obvious what he meant. In some markets, homes are bought and sold
before they are even finished. Double-digit annual price increases are common. And in April, both new and
existing home sales jumped to all-time record highs, pushing toward the fifth consecutive year of annual
records.
Nodding their heads with caffeinated vigor, bubble-believers gathered around the espresso machine and said
"I told you so -- even Greenspan thinks the trend is unsustainable." These nattering nabobs expect a housing
collapse to take down the U.S. economy. But excessive pessimism is unwarranted: Fears of a housing
bubble are overblown.
Housing is a complex market. It is affected by demographics, taxes, wealth, interest rates, regulation, the
economy, and commodity prices. Each of these has played a role in boosting demand for housing or holding
back supply. This has affected both market activity and prices in recent years. Understanding these trends is
important to any analysis.
-- Demographics -- Roughly 80% of all people between the ages of 50 and 64 own their own homes, versus
70.6% of people aged 35-49, and 69% of all Americans. The number of people in the U.S. aged 50-64
jumped from 35.3 million (13.3% of the population) in 1996 to 49.4 million (17.2% of population) in 2005. No
wonder housing is strong. As the population ages, homeownership rates rise -- it's as simple as that.
In addition, the baby boomer bulge is creating a great deal of churning in the housing market. Empty-nesters
are selling the home they raised their family in and moving to less time-consuming digs like condos, or buying
a second home. In many areas, older housing stock is bought only to be torn down and replaced.
-- Taxes -- Prior to 1997, homeowners 55 years of age or older were allowed a once-in-a-lifetime, tax-free
gain of up to $125,000 upon selling a home. But taxes could be avoided by rolling any capital gain from a
home sale into a new house. The result was a massive increase in pent-up home equity.
This equity was released in 1997, when President Clinton signed tax legislation that allowed couples to sell a
home they had lived in for at least two years and take up to $500,000 in gains tax free. Given the deductibility
of mortgage interest, housing is probably the least-taxed investment available to U.S. citizens. Tax something
less and more of it is created.
-- Wealth -- Just as the post-WWII economic boom led to two-car families, the wealth-creating boom of the
1980s and '90s is spawning new homeowners and many two-home families. All that pent-up housing wealth
created by baby boomers over the past 30 years had to go somewhere.
While many believe that irresponsible borrowing is creating a bubble in housing, this is not necessarily true.
At the end of 2004, U.S. households owned $17.2 trillion in housing assets, an increase of 18.1% (or $2.6
trillion) from the third quarter of 2003. Over the same five quarters, mortgage debt (including home equity
lines) rose $1.1 trillion to $7.5 trillion. The result: a $1.5 trillion increase in net housing equity over the past 15
months.
-- Interest Rates and Fed Policy -- After realizing that it had tightened excessively in 1999 and 2000, creating
deflationary pressures, the Fed cut the federal-funds rate 11 times in 2001, eventually pushing it to a 45-year
low of 1%. Using the Consumer Price Index (CPI), the real federal-funds rate has been below zero for more
than two years -- the most accommodative monetary policy since the mid-'70s.

--- Page 22 ---
Page 22 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Because productivity in home building has not increased as rapidly as in other industries, and regulation and
land restrictions have reduced available supply in some areas, an outward shift in the demand curve due to
easy money and demographics has lifted housing prices more than overall consumer prices. The average
price per square foot of a new home jumped 43.5% between 1992 and 2003. The CPI, excluding housing
costs, increased 27.2% during the same years. Housing prices have increased roughly 1.5% faster than
overall consumer prices -- a sign of strong demand and rising commodity prices, but hardly a bubble.
The Fed has also helped lift housing activity by working hard to hold down long-term interest rates. When he
was a Fed governor, Ben Bernanke urged the Fed to use its public communications to help manage the bond
market. He argued that if the Fed could lower the markets' expectations of the future path of short-term
interest rates, this would pull the entire yield curve lower. This thinking was the birthplace of the term
"measured." By telegraphing slow and steady rate hikes, the Fed has engineered low long-term rates.
Artificially low long-term interest rates have helped fuel strength in housing.
We suspect that Chairman Greenspan has been surprised by the bond markets' response to his use of the
word "froth." Many believe that the Fed waited too long to hike rates in response to "irrational exuberance" in
the equity market, so by raising the possibility of "local bubbles" in housing it would seem rational for the
market to expect the Fed to address this issue more quickly.
But the market reacted in the opposite direction. Following his "froth" speech, long-term bond yields fell
sharply as if the market expects the Fed to slow its rate hikes. This, however, would be a mistake. The stock
market exuberance of the late '90s occurred during a period of falling gold prices and disinflation or deflation.
Recent robust housing markets have occurred during a time of rising gold prices and growing rates of
inflation. The Fed is creating an environment where bubbles can exist today; this was not true in the late
'90s.
The good news is that demographic, tax and economic factors explain most, if not all, of the
greater-than-expected activity in housing. Moreover, the age groups with the largest propensity to own homes
will continue to make up a greater share of the population for the next 10 to 20 years. Adjusting for
demographics, it is virtually impossible to believe a nationwide housing bubble exists. Nonetheless, if the Fed
does stop hiking rates at current levels, a bubble could easily form. The best course of action for the Fed is to
ignore the housing market altogether. History has shown that the Fed makes its worst mistakes when it takes
its eye off inflation. Every significant decline in housing has occurred during a recession and the worst
slumps in housing happened in the stop-and-go, stagflationary, era of the '70s and early '80s.
To avoid repeating those days again, the Fed must boost rates enough to head off any further upswing in
inflation. Doing so may remove the monetary stimulus to housing, but what appears clear is that it won't
change the very positive trends already in place.
---
Mr. Wesbury is Chief Investment Strategist with Claymore Advisors, Inc.

Trinity Industries and MPS Gain
Small-Stock Focus
Trinity Industries and MPS Gain
By Mohammed Hadi Dow Jones Newswires
587 words
27 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- After taking a day off, small stocks resumed their move higher, with the Russell 2000 Index
and Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 climbing to their highest levels since early April and outperforming the
large-cap indexes.
As investors renewed their confidence in the economy, industrial-equipment companies advanced. Leading
small stocks was Trinity Industries (NYSE) of Dallas, which rose 90 cents, or 3.2%, to $28.84 after the
company signed an $82 million order for barge units and said the business appears to be in the beginning of
a recovery. Mining and construction equipment maker Manitowoc (NYSE) added 93 cents, or 2.4%, to 40.24,
while Astec Industries gained 43 cents, or 2.2%, to 20.28.
Gold-mining stocks fell, along with dollar alternatives like gold, as the dollar gained ground. Bema Gold
(Amex) lost six cents, or 3%, to 1.96, and Iamgold (Amex) fell 17 cents, or 2.6%, to 6.37.
Ned Riley, chief investment officer of Riley Asset Management, said the market's recent moves higher are the
result of "the silver lining in the soft patch," which is the expectation that slowing economic growth will inspire
the Federal Reserve to stop raising interest rates, he said. "They are contemplating or considering that the
Fed is going to be complete in the [rate increase] process by the end of the summer," Mr. Riley said.
The Russell 2000 Index gained 8.30 points, or 1.4%, to 614.70, the S&P SmallCap 600 Index added 4.34, or
1.4%, to 322.04, and the Nasdaq Composite Index of large- and small-cap stocks increased 21.12, or 1%, to
2071.24.
Thanks to a better-than-expected jobless-claims report, staffing and job-placement companies fared well.
Resources Connection rose 82 cents, or 4.3%, to 20.09, Hudson Highland Group rose 54 cents, or 3.6%, to
15.52, and MPS Group (NYSE) rose 29 cents, or 3.2%, to 9.36.
Home builders were the session's best-performing industry group after large- cap Toll Brothers said
second-quarter earnings more than doubled and beat analysts' expectations. Among small stocks, Orleans
Homebuilders (AMEX) added 90 cents, or 4.2%, to 22.50, Comstock Homebuilding gained 61 cents, or 2.7%,
to 23.19, and Brookfield Homes (NYSE) rose 76 cents, or 1.6%, to 47.01.
Steel producers continued their recent back and forth, posting sharp gains after leading the decliners on
Wednesday. AK Steel Holding gained 46 cents, or 6.6%, to 7.48, Oregon Steel Mills rose 96 cents, or 6%, to
16.98, and Ryerson Tull added 39 cents, or 2.7%, to 15.11, all on the New York Stock Exchange.
Wind River Systems surged 2.97, or 22%, to 16.45. The Alameda, Calif., software company posted fiscal
first-quarter earnings that beat analysts' views and boosted the lower end of its earnings forecast for the year
ending in January.
EFJ fell 69 cents, or 9%, to 7.01. The Irving, Texas, wireless-communications company said it will sell
another six million common shares and use the proceeds for general corporate purposes, business
development and research. It may also buy businesses and other assets.
Possis Medical rose 1.57, or 18%, to 10.44. Two analysts raised their ratings on the Minneapolis
medical-device company, which reported earnings on Wednesday, saying the company's new- product
pipeline is encouraging.

Moving Up: Challenges to the American Dream --- Rich vs. Richer: In Palm Beach, The Old Money Isn't Having a Ball --- Influx of New Wealth...
Moving Up: Challenges to the American Dream --- Rich vs. Richer: In Palm Beach, The Old Money
Isn't Having a Ball --- Influx of New Wealth Sparks Spat Over Red Cross Event; Inheritance's Smaller
Role --- A 1930s Landmark Is Razed
By Robert Frank
2,387 words
20 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
THE NUMBER of U.S. households with a net worth of more than $10 million in 1989 was 203,000. A
page-one article Friday about the wealthy incorrectly said there were 65,000 such households.
(WSJ May 25, 2005)
(END)
[Third in a Series]
PALM BEACH, Fla. -- For nearly a half-century, the Red Cross Ball was the most prestigious party for old
Palm Beach society. Then Simon Fireman took over.
The black-tie ball, founded by the late cereal heiress and society queen Marjorie Merriweather Post, brought
together the island's upper-crust families to drink, dance and donate money at the famed Breakers resort.
Foreign ambassadors flew in from Washington every year, as well as occasional royalty from Europe.
This year, Mr. Fireman, a Massachusetts native who made a fortune from inflatable pool toys, pledged
$750,000 to the local Red Cross and was named ball chairman. The Palm Beach newcomer moved the party
from the Breakers to Donald Trump's flashy Mar-a-Lago club. Regis Philbin came, along with hundreds of
out-of-town friends of Mr. Fireman. During his speech, Mr. Fireman boasted that he raised more money and
had more foreign ambassadors attending than previous balls.
Many of Palm Beach's top socialites boycotted the event, going instead to the Breakers for a competing ball
that benefited the International Center for Missing and Exploited Children.
"People here are worried that they now have to deal with a powerful force," says Mr. Fireman, sipping a
scotch with ice near the pool at Mar-a-Lago on a recent afternoon. Says Diana Ecclestone, the ball's previous
chairwoman: "Mr. Fireman's behavior isn't what most people in Palm Beach are willing to tolerate."
The battle of the balls highlights an escalating tug-of-war between old and new money that's playing out in
traditional blue-blood communities around the country. The number of superwealthy in the U.S. has surged,
with 430,000 households now worth more than $10 million. That's up from 65,000, adjusted for inflation, in
1989. In 2001, the top 1% of Americans ranked by net worth controlled 33% of all personal assets. As the
nouveaux riches buy their way into high society, they're increasingly clashing with an older elite that largely
lives off inherited money.
A study of the Forbes 400 list of super-rich Americans by Arthur Kennickell, senior economist with the Federal
Reserve in Washington, D.C., found that about half the people on the 2001 list weren't on the list in 1989. For
the nation's richest 1%, inherited wealth accounted for only 9% of their net worth in 2001, down from 23% in
1989, according to a study by New York University economist Edward Wolff.
The rapid shift in the composition of the tiny sliver of wealthiest Americans is striking because the amount of
U.S. class mobility overall hasn't significantly changed for the last three decades, according to economists.
The chance that a poor child will make it into the upper-middle class -- or that a rich child will fall down to the
middle class -- has stayed about the same, and some studies suggest mobility in the U.S. is less than in
continental Europe and Canada.

--- Page 33 ---
Page 33 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It's not that the old rich have become appreciably poorer, although taxes, inflation and offspring take their
toll. Rather, the new wealth among entrepreneurs has leapfrogged inherited money. Bill Gates's $48 billion
net worth is more than twice the Rockefeller family's current fortune.
The influx of new money has touched off disputes over values and status in places like Palm Beach, the
winter playground for the rich founded by oil magnate Henry Flagler in the 1890s. The 13-mile-long barrier
island's earliest residents -- Vanderbilts, Carnegies and Phippses -- were upstarts challenging the shipping
and trading fortunes of Europe. In the century that followed, however, pedigree was paramount. Even the
island's famous scandals, like the 1983 divorce trial of newspaper heiress Roxanne Pulitzer, involved
blue-blood names.
In recent years, more Latin Americans, Asians and Russians have moved to Palm Beach. The town, formerly
a haven for retirees and vacationers, has attracted entrepreneurs who run their companies at poolside and
type on their BlackBerries at charity balls. "I don't even golf," says George Cloutier, owner of a
management-consulting firm that he runs from his new beachside mansion.
The cost of living on Palm Beach has soared, pricing out many old families. Top homes are now selling for
$20 million to $30 million. The streets are clogged with Bentley GTs and Rolls-Royce Phantoms, replacing
the Volvos and Volkswagens favored by old money. Many of Palm Beach's founding families have died off or
moved out. Dina Merrill, the daughter of Red Cross ball founder Ms. Post, now stays in a condominium
apartment near the Breakers. The family sold its legendary estate, Mar-a-Lago, in 1985 to Mr. Trump, who
turned it into a country club.
Today's Palm Beach boldfaced names include Sydell Miller, the co-founder of the Matrix line of hair products
now owned by L'Oreal SA of France; Howard Kessler, a Boston native who pioneered the concept of the
affinity credit card; Netscape founder Jim Clark and pop singer Rod Stewart. Unlike the Bath &Tennis Club
and other traditional Palm Beach clubs, Mar-a-Lago welcomes just about anyone willing to pay the $150,000
entry price and $7,200 annual dues.
Even Democrats are moving in. Mr. Kessler and his wife, Michele, raised money at a dinner last year for
presidential candidate John Kerry.
Some of the biggest fights between old money and new have been over real estate -- Palm Beach's biggest
status symbol. Since 1997, more than 100 historic structures have been torn down on the island, according to
an architectural study. Ms. Miller, known locally as the "shampoo lady," razed a well-known neoclassical
mansion to build her 37,000-square-foot, $100 million home. The legendary Phipps estate has been broken
up into more than a dozen faux-Mediterranean mansions.
Last year, a public storm erupted over one of the island's most famous mansions, known as Four Winds. In
2003, Stephen A. Schwartzman, co-founder of the New York investment boutique Blackstone Group, bought
Four Winds for $20.5 million. Built for E.F. Hutton, the financier and former husband of Ms. Post, the
low-slung home with its white wooden siding and green shutters epitomized the Palm Beach estates of the
1930s and 1940s. It was also a historic landmark.
Mr. Schwartzman wanted the 13,000-square-foot home expanded. His team filed plans with the town's
landmarks commission for a "renovation and restoration" that would add a second floor and a servants' wing.
The plans didn't explain the details of how the work would be done, and the commission never asked.
On July 12, 2004, Four Winds was demolished. Old Palm Beachers driving by the site were stunned to find
the house had nearly vanished, and the town's then-mayor called it a "bitter, bitter pill."
The town set up a panel to investigate. Mr. Schwartzman's architect said the contractors discovered during
construction that the home's walls were too weak to support the additions and had to be rebuilt. Mr.
Schwartzman explained to town officials that he'd given them all the necessary information and had received
all the relevant approvals, and the panel agreed. He promised to use the original walls and materials from
Four Winds -- which were saved, labeled and stored -- to rebuild according to its original design, with the
expansions.
"In hindsight, maybe he could have been more forthcoming, and we could have asked more questions," says
Eugene Pandula, the commission's chairman. The town has since implemented new approval rules.
The debate over the Red Cross ball has been far more personal. With his gold bracelets, bright ties and
fondness for publicity, the 79-year-old Mr. Fireman says he's different from traditional Palm Beachers.
Born in the blue-collar Boston neighborhood of Dorchester, Mr. Fireman is the son of a Russian immigrant
who owned a small marine-gear company. In 1970, with $1,000 in savings, the younger Mr. Fireman started
his own company, called Aqua-Leisure. The privately held company grew into a top producer of inflatable

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
water toys and other pool gear. Now on his second marriage, Mr. Fireman has estates in Palm Beach and
Hyannisport, Mass., just down the beach from the Kennedys.
In 1996, he pleaded guilty to making illegal contributions to Bob Dole's presidential campaign. He spent six
months in home confinement and paid a $1 million fine, plus a $5 million fine paid by his company. Mr.
Fireman says the case against him was a political attack by the Clinton administration. In contrast to Palm
Beach's old money, he's not shy about advertising his virtues: He recently commissioned an entire issue of
Palm Beach Society Magazine about himself, with the cover line reading, "Simon Fireman: Innovator, Leader,
Humanitarian."
He's also a high-profile donor. Pulling a spreadsheet from his blazer pocket over a Cobb salad lunch at the
Mar-a-Lago, Mr. Fireman says he has given away more than $20 million since 1995, much of it to Palm
Beach causes. He frequently stands up during charity events and announces donations of $10,000 or
$100,000.
"When you see sadness, pain and suffering I want to rise up and I want to help," he says.
For more than 20 years, the Red Cross ball was run by Sue Whitmore, heir to the Listerine fortune. Of the
more than 100 charity balls held in Palm Beach every winter, the Red Cross has been among the most
prestigious because of its exclusive guest list. Traditionally the chairman (almost always a woman) would
invite 200 to 300 of her friends, charge them $200 or so a ticket, and throw a nicely catered party with a swing
band. Most years, the ball raised $100,000 or less.
In 2001, the chairmanship passed to Diana Ecclestone, 38, a former county commissioner's aide who married
wealthy developer Llwyd Ecclestone. Ms. Ecclestone was something of a transitional figure, making the ball
more friendly to big-giving newcomers while retaining the support of many tradition-minded Palm Beach
doyennes.
Ms. Ecclestone created a series of VIP events preceding the ball. Donors who gave $20,000 got to go to a
dinner weeks earlier at a Palm Beach mansion, a fashion show by designer Oscar de la Renta and a later
dinner at jeweler Van Cleef &Arpels, catered by restaurateur Daniel Boulud's Cafe Boulud. Last year the Red
Cross took in more than $1.4 million from the event.
Other balls in town followed suit with big-donor events. As ticket prices soared, so did spending on gowns
and jewelry. Mr. Cloutier, the consulting firm owner, splurged to buy a $35,000 necklace for his girlfriend to
wear to a ball two years ago. They arrived to find five other women wearing the same necklace, he says. Ms.
Ecclestone wore a $4 million diamond-studded tiara to the 2002 ball, lent by Van Cleef &Arpels.
Ms. Ecclestone's changes rankled some in the old guard. "They'd say, `I have to give $25,000 just to be
involved in a charity?' " she says. And she feuded with local executives of the Red Cross over perceptions
that she was snubbing Red Cross staff and taking too much control of the ball. Weeks after the 2004 ball, the
Red Cross told her she could no longer be chairwoman. Dean Dimke, executive director of the Greater Palm
Beach chapter of the American Red Cross, says Ms. Ecclestone did a "wonderful" job but the chapter wanted
to open up the chairman's position to others.
Many of Palm Beach's older families rallied to Ms. Ecclestone's side, feeling the Red Cross was acting in an
undignified way, and refused to give to the 2005 ball.
Mr. Fireman stepped into the breach. He wrote a check for $750,000 and was named Red Cross ball
chairman for the next two years. Mr. Fireman moved the 2005 ball to Mar-a-Lago, which had just built a
marble-and-gold ballroom modeled after Louis XIV's ballroom at Versailles. More than 700 people attended,
many from Boston, Washington and Boca Raton. After a dinner of lamb chops, potatoes Anna and
asparagus, Mr. Fireman took the stage and touted his results.
"We have 16 ambassadors this year; last year they had six," he said. "We raised $2 million; last year they
raised $1 million."
Ms. Ecclestone was furious, saying Mr. Fireman had "trashed me in front of everyone in Palm Beach." She
points out that many of her ambassadors were European, while "he had Syria and Guyana."
Mr. Fireman says he was just stating facts and honoring those who had worked hard to raise money. He also
says Ms. Ecclestone never gave much of her own money to the Red Cross. She says she gave $25,000 and
many hours of her time each year.
"Palm Beach can be a closed society," says Mr. Fireman. "You're not allowed into certain inner circles." He
says Ms. Ecclestone pressured Palm Beach's big-money socialites to skip the ball and attend the rival event
at the Breakers the same night.

--- Page 35 ---
Page 35 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Ecclestone denies the charge but concedes she bears some responsibility for making the tone of Palm
Beach society less genteel than it once was. "Maybe I'm guilty of making the money more important," she
says. "But just because you give a lot of money doesn't mean you can stand up and make a jerk of yourself."
---

(WSJ May 23, 2005)

Stocks Advance As Fears Abate, Hope Returns
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Advance As Fears Abate, Hope Returns
By E.S. Browning
402 words
20 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS TURNED in a fourth-consecutive session of gains this week, as fears of a market blowup continued
to fade and hopes for the economy took their place.
The Dow Jones Industrial Averaged added 28.74 points, or 0.27%, finishing at 10493.19. The four-day gain of
353.07 points is its strongest in six months, although the blue-chip average remains down 2.7% this year.
Reflecting how bullish investors have become, the gains came despite news that wasn't all that positive. A
gauge of manufacturing in the Philadelphia area fell to a two-year low and the Conference Board's index of
leading indicators declined in April, its fourth-consecutive pullback.
But the number of new unemployment claims fell a week ago -- generally seen as a sign of economic
strength. The fears that roiled markets last week, involving the risk of a blowup at a hedge fund, a loosely
regulated private-investment pool, continued to fade.
"I just think it is the seeping-through into people's minds that things really are pretty good," with inflation low,
the economy growing and corporate profits strong, said Alfred Kugel, chief investment strategist at Chicago
investment-management firm Atlantic Trust/Stein Roe.
Reassuring investors was a decline in crude-oil futures to less than $47 a barrel, down 33 cents at $46.92 on
the New York Mercantile Exchange. That is the lowest finish since February and an 18% decline from the
22-year high of $57.27 hit last month.
Bond prices pulled back a little following recent gains, pushing yields slightly higher. The dollar resumed its
rise and gold fell.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index advanced 0.47%, or 5.52 points, to 1191.08, down just 1.7% for 2005.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.59%, or 11.93 points, to 2042.58, still down 6% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose and bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 11/32, or $3.4375 for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield up to 4.113%. The 30-year bond was down 8/32 to yield 4.445%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 107.65 yen, up from 106.81; the euro fell against the
dollar to $1.2634 from $1.2681.

Three-Day Gain In Dow Average Is Best Since November
Wednesday's Markets
Three-Day Gain In Dow Average Is Best Since November
By E.S. Browning
663 words
19 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A FLASH FLOOD of good news on inflation, profits, oil and economic growth propelled the Dow Jones
Industrial Average to its second 100-point gain in three days and its strongest three-day performance since
November.
Amid renewed hopes for a continuation of the "Goldilocks" economy -- not too hot, not too cold -- the Dow
industrials finished at their highest level in more than a month, rising 132.57 points, or 1.28%, to 10464.45.
It was their third-strongest point or percentage gain of the year and left the industrials up 324.33 points, or
3.2%, over the week's first three days. The blue-chip average is still down 3% for 2005. It has turned in 12
100-point moves in the past 26 sessions, five gains and seven declines.
"The market seems kind of manic, one day up 100, the next day down 100," said Todd Clark, head of listed
trading at San Francisco brokerage firm Wells Fargo Securities.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, with its many volatile technology stocks, is back at a level it hadn't seen in two
months. It jumped 1.32%, or 26.50 points, to 2030.65, still off 6.7% this year.
Investors were pleased to see Treasury-bond prices advance in tandem with stocks, as hope spread that the
tame inflation numbers would permit the Federal Reserve to avoid any sharp increase in interest rates.
Some investors have begun to speculate that the Fed might stop raising rates before long. Lower rates are
good for both stocks and bonds.
Higher bond prices mean lower bond yields, and the yield of the 10-year Treasury note fell to 4.073%, its
lowest level since Feb. 9. Bond yields help determine a variety of market interest rates, and low yields tend
to lower costs for businesses and consumers alike.
The latest government inflation report showed that, excluding food and oil, consumer prices were unchanged
in April -- a sign that the year's rise in oil prices hasn't spilled over heavily into other sectors. That was doubly
positive because oil prices now are falling.
Crude-oil futures yesterday pulled back $1.72 to $47.25 a barrel, following an Energy Department report that
U.S. oil and gasoline supplies remain abundant. That marked a three-month low for oil and the fifth
consecutive session below $50. Crude still is up 8.8% this year, but it is 18% below its 22-year high of $57.27
hit in April.
On top of that came an overnight report that Chinese industrial production rose faster than expected in April,
which bolstered hopes that the world economy remains strong. And Hewlett-Packard reported
better-than-expected quarterly results. That helped boost technology stocks and reminded investors that
first-quarter corporate profits in general have strongly surpassed expectations.
Hopes for modest interest-rate increases hurt the dollar, which benefits from higher rates. Gold, which
investors often use as a bet against the dollar, rebounded, though it remains down 3.7% this year.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 1%, or 11.76 points, to 1185.56, also a one-month high. It now is
down only 2% for 2005. Trading volume was heavy.
One reason for the sharpness of the day's gain is somewhat artificial, having to do with short sellers, who bet
against the market by selling borrowed stock. The sudden rebound forced many of them to unwind their bets
by buying.
In major U.S. market action:

Stocks and bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 14/32, or $4.375 for each $1,000 invested, pushing
the yield down to 4.073%. The 30-year bond was up 28/32 to yield 4.431%.
The dollar weakened. It traded at 106.81 yen, down from 107.54, while the euro rose to $1.2681 from
$1.2599.

AK Steel and Big Lots Rise, but Curagen, Telik Fall
Small-Stock Focus
AK Steel and Big Lots Rise, but Curagen, Telik Fall
By Mohammed Hadi Dow Jones Newswires
647 words
18 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After a late-session reversal, small stocks extended the gains made on Monday as buyers hunted for
bargains. Trading activity, though, was again light.
Cyclical stocks such as steel companies, which have been out of favor in recent days, were among the
session's best performers. AK Steel Holding (NYSE) rose 48 cents, or 1.3%, to $7.07, and Commercial
Metals (NYSE) added 83 cents, or 3.5%, to 24.27. Also, Canadian steel processor Novamerican Steel
jumped 3.76, or 12%, to 36.28 after Jefferies &Co. initiated coverage of the stock at "hold," saying it is a
"high-quality company" within its industry group.
At the same time, the biotechnology stocks that have posted recent gains gave some back. Curagen fell 18
cents, or 4.9%, to 3.52, even as the company said early clinical trials of one of its drugs showed that it is well
tolerated. Telik fell 72 cents, or 4.4%, to 15.60, even as it too released positive data from an early trial of its
lung-cancer treatment. Medarex dropped 37 cents, or 4.7%, to 7.53.
Richard Madigan, global market strategist at J.P. Morgan Private Bank, said the recently "bumpy markets"
are a result of rising interest rates and volatility. Investors are "trying to find the new fair value [for stocks]
over the next 12 months to 18 months," he said. As that process continues, "I think we are going to do a lot of
the dancing that we've seen the markets do in the last week, in the next month or so," he said.
The Russell 2000 index rose 3.56 points, or 0.6%, to 595.27. The S&P SmallCap 600 added 1.89, or 0.6%, to
312.04, and the Nasdaq Composite Index of large- and small-cap stocks advanced 9.72, or 0.5%, to 2004.15.
Retailers fared well after several companies, including large caps Home Depot and Staples, beat
expectations with their quarterly earnings. Among small caps, Big Lots (NYSE) rose 1.58, or 14%, to 12.75
after the discount retailer also beat expectations and reiterated its second-quarter earnings forecast. Sports
Authority (NYSE) added 75 cents, or 2.7%, to 28.43, and 99 Cents Only Stores (NYSE) rose 58 cents, or
5.4%, to 11.29.
Some small auto-parts stocks fell after small-cap Collins &Aikman became the latest company to file for
Chapter 11 bankruptcy-court protection. Visteon fell 10 cents, or 2.8%, to 3.44, Modine Manufacturing fell 23
cents, or 0.8%, to 28.34, and American Axle &Manufacturing Holdings lost three cents, or 0.2%, to 18.87, all
three on the Big Board.
Gold and silver futures closed higher, and precious-metals mining stocks followed. Coeur D'Alene Mines
(NYSE) rose 10 cents, or 3.6%, to 2.87, and Lihir Gold's American depositary shares gained 18 cents, or
1.1%, to 15.96.
Willbros Group (NYSE) plunged 4.92, or 31%, to 11. The Houston oil-services company issued a first-quarter
earnings forecast that was below analysts' expectations, said it will restate and lower earnings for 2003 and
the first three quarters of 2004, and said it expects to default on financial covenants. The stock was
downgraded by several analysts.
SonicWall increased 41 cents, or 7.8%, to 5.70. The Sunnyvale, Calif., network-security products company
swung to a first-quarter profit as reduced expenses offset a drop in revenue. The results, excluding certain
charges, beat analysts' expectations.
Jo-Ann Stores (NYSE) fell 2.99, or 11%, to 25.09. The Hudson, Ohio, fabric and craft retailer said first-quarter
earnings fell from a year earlier, and the company projected a loss for the second quarter.

Stocks Decline As Wal-Mart Sparks Worries
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Decline As Wal-Mart Sparks Worries
By E.S. Browning
451 words
13 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A WEAK PROFIT forecast from Wal-Mart Stores combined with falling oil prices to fuel worries about the
economy, sending stocks down. But the dollar surged to a six-month high against the euro, as investors
concluded that the U.S. may hold up better than some foreign economies.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 110.77 points, or 1.08%, to 10189.48, its lowest finish since April 28
and its sharpest point or percentage drop in two weeks. The blue-chip average is down 5.5% for 2005.
"Everything they can take out of the ground is getting absolutely hammered -- meaning oil and gold," said
Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md. Stocks linked to
raw materials and basic industries, such as Alcoa and Exxon Mobil, led the declines. The rising dollar makes
their products more expensive world-wide.
Investors use gold and other commodities as a bet against the dollar; as commodities fell, traders shifted into
dollar-denominated securities, strengthening the U.S. currency and U.S. Treasury bonds.
The $1.91 fall in crude-oil futures to $48.54 on the New York Mercantile Exchange left oil at a three-month
low. Instead of celebrating, some investors began to worry that it could signal a global economic slowdown.
Gold fell $5.60, or 1.3%, to $421.60 an ounce, the sharpest drop since March and a 3.6% decline so far this
year.
Investors were talking about European staff cuts announced by International Business Machines and Ford
Motor's debt downgrade by Moody's Investors Service. Although strong April retail sales initially helped
stocks, some investors began to worry that the news could make the Federal Reserve raise interest rates
more, further burdening the economy.
The S&P 500 index fell 1%, or 11.75 points, to 1159.36, down 4.3% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index
declined 0.39%, or 7.67 points, to 1963.88, off 9.7% for 2005.
Traders got good news late in the day: Dell jumped 2.7% in after-hours trading when its quarterly profit,
released after regular hours, matched expectations and it reported an April business pickup.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated and bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield down to 4.176%. The 30-year bond was up 15/32 to yield 4.521%.
The dollar strengthened. It traded at 106.77 yen, up from 105.78, while the euro fell against the dollar to
$1.2706 from $1.2804.

The Government Might Help You Make Money!
The Government Might Help You Make Money!
By James Grant
1,127 words
11 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
News coverage of the probable return of the 30-year Treasury bond must seem strangely overwrought to the
hundreds of millions of Americans who don't trade fixed-income securities for a living. Who even remembered
that the so-called long bond was put out to pasture?
But it was, on Oct. 31, 2001. The Treasury official in front of the microphones acknowledged that the federal
budget was in the red. And it might so remain in the short run, he said, before adding unpresciently (the
economy was then softening and the war on terror only beginning): "Even if this happens we expect that the
federal government will return to surpluses in the coming years."
With surpluses nowhere in sight, with long-term interest rates low and with the demand for long-dated
investment assets high, the administration has every reason to resume issuing long bonds (a decision is due
in August). And the thoughtful investor has good reason not to buy them.
All have reason to pay attention. The government's borrowing strategy touches on every vital financial issue:
inflation, interest rates, pension reform and the U.S. international financial position. And it shines a light on
the reduced state of a different kind of 30-year debt instrument. As the Treasury is preparing to lock in
historically low long-term borrowing costs, homeowners are increasingly borrowing for shorter terms, and at
adjustable rates. In the past two years, the share of new mortgage originations bearing adjustable rates
surged to 60% from 20%. On the question of how to borrow, and for how long, someone is right, and
someone is wrong. Astoundingly, it appears that the government, not the people, has the better grasp of the
situation in 2005.
At intervals, borrowers and lenders agree to do business not just for years but over the long sweep of time.
Typically, the mood so strikes them in times of perceived monetary stability or ultra-low prevailing interest
rates, or both. Thus, in the 19th century, the British government, whose pound sterling was the world's top
monetary brand, could borrow in perpetuity. In the U.S., ultra-long-dated issues enjoyed a vogue in the early
1900s. Confident in the dollar (anchored by the Gold Standard Act of 1900) and complacent about interest
rates (falling since 1873), creditors thought nothing of lending until kingdom come, or, in the extreme case of
West Shore Railroad, the year 2361. They miscalculated. No sooner was the gold dollar enshrined on the
statute books than a bear bond market began. Interest rates rose, and bond prices fell, for the next 20 years.
All markets are cyclical. Interest-rate cycles are different only in that they tend to be especially long-trending.
Thus, bond yields fell from 1873 to 1900, rose from 1900 until 1920, fell from 1920 to 1946, rose from 1946 to
1981, fell from 1981 to 2003 -- and now, I believe, are embarked on a new upswing.
This is a minority opinion. The world over, investors clamor for long-dated government bonds. In the U.K.,
yields on 30-year gilts are actually lower than those on the 10-year models (4.49% vs. 4.56%). In France, a
new issue of 50-year government bonds was hugely oversubscribed, even though the coupon was just 4%
and the euro, the currency in which the securities are denominated, has existed in non-electronic form for
less than four years. Germany, too, is weighing a half-century bond issue after yields on the 10-year Bund
touched 3.33%, lowest since 1896, the Financial Times reports.
Why this insatiable demand for bonds paying yield by the thimbleful? Demographics is a popular pretext. The
argument goes that fiduciaries are unprepared for the imminent wave of baby-boom retirements. Pension
funds have invested too little, and without due regard to the lengthening life expectancy of prospective
pensioners. Impetus for a better matching of assets and liabilities could enlarge demand for long-duration
bonds by $2 trillion to $6 trillion, the Treasury's own advisers point out. But there are only $345 billion of
Treasury securities in existence with 15 or more years until maturity. These are fearful and powerful numbers.
They would have to be to seduce once-cynical investors into buying the low-yielding bonds of governments
that print the inflation-prone currencies in which the bonds are quoted. Following the great inflation of the
'70s, abused creditors vowed "Never again." Rather than be coerced into financing the next inflation, they

--- Page 48 ---
Page 48 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
would exercise their franchise. They would, by selling bonds, drive up interest rates, stopping inflation
before it could start.
It's not the way it's worked. Foreign central banks happily finance the vast and growing gap between what the
U.S. produces and what it consumes. On Oct. 31, 2001, foreign official creditors held some $738 billion of
U.S. government securities; at last report, they owned $1.398 trillion. Naturally, cessation of long-bond
issuance has shortened the average length of the public debt. But to this potentially worrying fact, the foreign
creditors enter no protest.
What would they protest against? Officially, inflation in the U.S. is hardly a blip. Yes, the Consumer Price
Index rose at an annual rate of 4.3% in the first three months of the year (vs. 3.3% for all of 2004). But, the
bond bulls claim, inflation at the millennium has become a virtual impossibility. With India and China newly
annexed to the world economy, the supply of goods and services will forever outrun the demand.
That is, until the dollar exchange rate buckles, or Chinese and Indian consumers decide that, like Americans,
they would rather spend than save. In the meantime, inflation in the U.S. takes the form of soaring house
prices. Credit has financed this bull market, as it does most, but in this case with a difference. More and more
American mortgages are adjustable rate. To swing the purchase of the fast-appreciating house of their
dreams, consumers increasingly prefer to pay a small premium over money-market interest rates rather than
to assume a bigger monthly payment at a fixed rate (they also prefer, increasingly, to pay only interest, with
little attempt to pay down principal). And because fewer mortgages are of the old, fixed-rate, 30-year kind,
there are that many fewer long-dated investments to buy. Hence the Treasury's long-bond trial balloon.
Only rarely does the U.S. government blaze a profitable financial trail for the American people to follow. Last
week's proposal might just be that rare occurrence.
---
Mr. Grant is the editor of Grant's Interest Rate Observer.

Malaysia Considers BAE Arms Deal --- British Defense Contractor Proposes Country Use Gold To Help Finance Purchases
Malaysia Considers BAE Arms Deal --- British Defense Contractor Proposes Country Use Gold To
Help Finance Purchases
By Leslie Lopez
808 words
10 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4B
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
KUALA LUMPUR, Malaysia -- The Malaysian government is considering an arms deal that calls for it to
commit part of the country's gold reserves to help finance the purchase of warships and other defense
equipment valued at roughly GBP 1 billion, or about $2 billion, from BAE Systems PLC.
The proposed use of Malaysia's gold holdings as collateral for the deal is highly unusual, according to
bankers and economists here. It could spark controversy in Malaysia, where the country's central bank
incurred losses of about $4 billion during the early 1990s using its reserves to trade in international currency
markets, a strategy that drew heavy criticism at the time.
Under the BAE-initiated proposal, the British defense contractor would arrange financing for the arms
purchases with a loan secured by gold certificates issued by the Malaysian government, according to people
familiar with the matter. Other aspects of the plan, such as the tenure and the details of the equipment
Malaysia intends to purchase, haven't been finalized. But people familiar with the proposal say the annual
interest rate on a gold-secured borrowing could be as low as 0.5%.
A senior aide to Malaysian Deputy Prime Minister Najib Abdul Razak, who serves as defense minister,
described the plan as "an unsolicited proposal that is being looked at" by the government. "No decision has
been taken, and whatever purchase of defense equipment [Malaysia enters into] will take into consideration
our financial position," the aide said.
A spokesman for BAE Systems in London acknowledged the company was in discussions with the Malaysian
government over its defense requirements, but declined to elaborate or to discuss the arms-financing
proposal.
According to Bank Negara, Malaysia's central bank and custodian of the nation's financial reserves, the
country's gold and foreign-exchange holdings at the end of 2004 exceeded 250 billion ringgit, or about $65
billion, enough to finance eight months of retained imports. The central bank doesn't provide a breakdown of
its reserve holdings by category, but should Kuala Lumpur approve the BAE proposal, the country would be
committing about 2.8% of its total reserves to fund the arms purchases from BAE.
Financial executives familiar with the financing proposal say the BAE plan is notable for its innovation.
Malaysian Prime Minister Abdullah Ahmad Badawi's government has been pushing to cut the country's
budget deficit and generally has avoided fresh commitments to big-ticket contracts and state-funded projects.
But BAE's suggested use of gold reserves as security for the arms purchase represents a novel way to
secure funding at low rates.
By comparison, the Malaysian government could expect to pay 0.75 percentage point above the interest rate
on U.S. Treasury notes -- or slightly less than 5% -- for a 10-year U.S. dollar sovereign-risk borrowing, the
financial executives say.
Still, because it is unconventional, BAE's financing proposal could prompt debate over management of
Malaysia's reserves. Bankers based in Kuala Lumpur and economists say they can't recall any instance
where a government has committed its gold reserves as security for borrowings to fund a commercial deal.
They say that the pledging of a country's gold holdings as security against borrowings usually happens when
governments are faced with a financial crisis.
Malaysia has had one bitter experience using its foreign reserves for unorthodox purposes. During the late
1980s, the Malaysian government under former Prime Minister Mahathir Mohamad approved a plan
permitting Bank Negara to use the country's then-rapidly mounting gold and foreign-currency reserves to
trade aggressively in currency markets in Asia, Europe and the U.S.

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While central banks routinely trade currency for hedging purposes, it is extremely unusual for banks to use
such trading with the goal of maximizing profits.
Bank Negara initially racked up substantial profits from its currency bets. Then in 1992, it misread the market
badly. Two years later, the central bank declared it had incurred losses of more than 15 billion ringgit from
currency trading.
Some analysts suggest that Malaysia could blunt potential criticism of the BAE plan if Kuala Lumpur adopted
a more transparent approach to its arms purchases, perhaps by calling for competitive bidding on defense
contracts. But that appears unlikely, because the government has long insisted that its defense procurements
must be conducted through private negotiations on the grounds of national security.
Malaysia's defense ministry has lobbied for more offshore patrol vessels to police its territorial waters.
The issue has become more urgent after recent problems surrounding a project by the Malaysian government
to build its own offshore patrol vessels through a joint venture between a local company, PSC Industries
Bhd., and ThyssenKrupp AG of Germany.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Prickly Issues on Tap for U.S.-Vietnam Visit
Trade and human rights likely will be the prickliest issues on the table when Vietnam's prime minister next
month becomes the first to visit the U.S. since communist tanks rolled into Saigon 30 years ago.
The planned June 21 meeting between Prime Minister Phan Van Khai and President Bush highlights how the
countries have established a normalized trading relationship -- albeit one with spats over quotas and tariffs --
since Washington lifted its trade embargo against Hanoi in 1994.
Vietnam needs U.S. support to achieve its goal of entering the World Trade Organization by year end.
Washington is pressing Hanoi for greater access to its markets for services and agricultural products, and
U.S. trade officials say Vietnam must pass about 30 new or amended laws before it qualifies to join the trade
body.
The longer membership is delayed, the longer Vietnam's textile exports to the U.S. will remain under quota
limits while major competitors, particularly China, have almost unfettered access to the U.S. market.
U.S. investments in Vietnam swelled to $2.6 billion in 2003, making it the fourth-biggest foreign investor that
year, the last full year for which data are available. Two-way trade reached $6.44 billion in 2004, up from $5.8
billion in 2003, despite Vietnamese resentment that the U.S. last yar imposed tariffs of 93% on Vietnamese
shrimp exports.
Still, there are sore points, the shrimp dispute chief among them. Moreover, while U.S. companies believe
Vietnam has potential as an investment site, they complain that corruption, red tape and frequent changes in
regulations are hampering their ventures. U.S. Ambassador Michael W. Marine recently noted how Ford
Motor Co. encountered an unexpected tax on new automobiles shortly after building a $100 million factory in
the country.
The U.S. is likely to raise several human-rights concerns during Mr. Khai's visit, notably Vietnam's restrictions
on religious groups and its repression of ethnic minorities.
-- James Hookway and Murray Hiebert
Philippine Lawmakers Open Door to VAT Rise
MANILA, Philippines -- Lawmakers have agreed to grant President Gloria Macapagal Arroyo the power to
raise the value-added tax rate -- a bid to end an impasse that has stalled passage of the bill. The measure is
the focal point of changes intended to balance the budget by 2010.
Jesli Lapus, chairman of the House of Representatives' Ways and Means Committee, said 24 of the 35
congressmen and six of the nine senators who make up the joint committee have signed a proposed
compromise bill that will allow Ms. Arroyo to raise the value-added tax rate next year.
Ms. Arroyo can increase the rate to 12% from 10% if value-added tax collection exceeds 2.8% of 2005 gross
domestic product or the budget deficit exceeds 1.5% of this year's GDP, Mr. Lapus said. GDP is the total
value of goods and services the nation produces.
Sen. Ralph Rector, chairman of the Senate Ways and Means Committee, acknowledged that conditions set
by Congress for Ms. Arroyo practically ensure that the value-added tax will be raised to 12% next year. "The
parameters are clear, but the bar is admittedly not that high," he said.

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The government has set a budget-deficit ceiling of 180 billion pesos ($3.33 billion) this year, equal to 3.6% of
GDP.
Value-added tax revisions, including the removal of exemptions now enjoyed by sectors such as power
generation and airlines, as well as raising the rate from 10%, are the main revenue-generating measures in
Ms. Arroyo's package of fiscal changes.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
Snow Says China Can Handle Yuan Revaluation
HARTFORD, Conn. -- China's financial system is ready to handle the adjustment to a flexible currency
system, U.S. Treasury Secretary John Snow said, keeping up the pressure on Beijing to let the yuan
strengthen.
"It clearly is time for the Chinese to act," Mr. Snow said yesterday following a speech to business leaders
here. "They've prepared the way, and made great strides with their financial system. Now is the time."
Chinese officials have said they intend to loosen the yuan's peg to the dollar -- a move that many expect
would result in a stronger Chinese currency -- but only after revamping the nation's ailing financial system.
Finance Minister Jin Renqing last week said speculation was running too high for Beijing to move immediately
without jeopardizing economic stability.
A delegation from China's central bank was in Washington yesterday for meetings at the Treasury
Department that Mr. Snow said were technical discussions.
The White House is under increasing pressure from Congress to become tougher with Beijing. U.S.
manufacturers complain the yuan is undervalued and unfairly cheapens Chinese imports.
Comments from Chinese officials give little reason to expect a near-term move on the currency, but analysts
who forecast a move sooner rather than later say China may use the scheduled expansion on May 18 of
onshore foreign-exchange trading as a trigger for loosening the currency. On that date, China will allow
onshore trading of eight new currency pairs.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
BRIEFLY:
DENVER -- The health problems of villagers near Newmont Mining Corp.'s former Minahasa gold mine can't
conclusively be linked to its operations, according to a study by the Indonesian Ministry of Health. Test results
showed there was no difference in the average values of exposure to heavy metals in the people suspected
to have diseases and those who didn't, the report summary said. Indonesian authorities and human-rights
groups have accused Newmont's local unit of dumping toxic levels of mercury and arsenic waste from its
operations into Buyat Bay. The company has denied the charges.

THE AMERICAS
Rising Inflation Persists in Brazil and Mexico
Signs of higher inflation in Brazil and Mexico dimmed hopes of a break in interest-rate increases.
In Mexico, inflation rose 0.36% in April, and 4.6% for the 12 months through April. That was higher than the
4.39% recorded for the year through March. The latest increase, largely coming from a jump in vegetable
prices, has put inflation even further beyond the bank's target range of 2% to 4% a year.
Core inflation, which excludes fresh fruit and vegetables, energy and education, rose only 0.21% in April,
however, bringing annual core inflation down to 3.46% from 3.61% at the end of March. It was the sixth
straight month of declines in annual core inflation.
Some economists think that given the decline in core inflation, the Bank of Mexico will hold off further
monetary tightening this month. Others, however, are forecasting further tightening from Mexico's central
bank Friday.
"The figure is high," said Eduardo Avila, an economist with consulting firm Prognosis. "Given that mid-term
inflation projections are still far from the [central bank's] target, I think monetary policy will be tightened on
Friday."

--- Page 54 ---
Page 54 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A year of monetary tightening by the central bank, coupled with rising U.S. rates, has led Mexican overnight
rates to practically double in the past year to about 9.8%.
In Brazil, meanwhile, inflation expectations continued to nudge higher yesterday. According to a
central-bank survey, markets now expect Brazil's benchmark IPCA inflation index to rise 6.3% in 2005 and
5.85% over the rolling 12-month period ahead. The central bank's target for 2005 inflation is 5.1% with a
tolerance band up to 7%.
The central bank has raised its benchmark Selic rate to 19.5% in a bid to contain inflation. Analysts now
believe the bank may have to maintain higher interest rates for longer than expected.
-- A Wall Street Journal News Roundup

Sector Extends Its Recent Rise, With Lift From Steel Dynamics
Small-Stock Focus
Sector Extends Its Recent Rise, With Lift From Steel Dynamics
By Karen Talley Dow Jones Newswires
564 words
9 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Small-stock indexes rose 3% last week, outperforming their large-cap counterparts after weeks of lagging
behind.
The positive week was capped off by modest gains Friday along with the overall market, after investors
received a stronger-than-expected employment report for April.
But the Friday gains ended up being mild because "economic news has been mixed so it's hard for people to
latch onto any one number and have it definitively turn the market," said Andy Brooks, head of equity trading
at T. Rowe Price.
In fact, real-estate investment trusts were tugged lower by the report on concerns its strength will work in
favor of higher interest rates and produce greater competition for the companies' dividends. NovaStar
Financial lost $1.37, or 3.4%, to $38.53, Redwood Trust fell 1.26, or 2.4%, to 51.89, and Washington Real
Estate Investment Trust lost 80 cents, or 2.6%, to 29.70, all on the New York Stock Exchange.
There was also action among metals stocks, with differing outcomes.
Steel stocks continued their comeback after a recent dip on fears of overcapacity and slower demand. Steel
Dynamics gained 45 cents, or 1.6%, to 28.07, AK Steel rose nine cents, or 1.2%, to 7.54, and Worthington
Industries advanced 38 cents, or 2.3%, to 16.81, the latter two on the New York Stock Exchange.
But gold stocks fell as the price of the precious metal declined. Coeur d'Alene Mines (NYSE) lost 10 cents, or
3%, to 3.29, Hecla Mining (NYSE) fell 15 cents, or 3%, to 4.80, and Cambior (Amex) dropped four cents, or
2.1%, to 1.86.
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks gained 0.88, or 0.15%, to 596.52. For the week, the index advanced
3%.
The S&P SmallCap 600 Index rose 0.92, or 0.3%, to 312.22 on Friday and 3% on the week. The Nasdaq
Composite Index rose 5.55, or 0.28%, to 1967.35 Friday. For the week, the Nasdaq advanced 2.4%.
UTStarcom plunged 3.21, or 31%, to 7.24. The Alameda, Calif., telecom equipment maker posted a slide in
first-quarter earnings and warned it will cut 17% of its work force because of slowing wireless business in
China.
Click Commerce jumped 4.94, or 44%, to 16.27. The Chicago software provider reported a tripling in
first-quarter profit and doubling in revenue from the year-earlier period.
Jamdat Mobile rose 6.82, or 39%, to 24.33. The Los Angeles wireless entertainment publisher issued robust
2005 and 2006 guidance, and analysts said the company is well-positioned to take advantage of growth in
the mobile gaming market.
SeaChange International lost 1.36, or 13%, to 8.97. The Maynard, Mass., digital video systems provider put
its first-quarter loss at less than a penny a share to a loss of three cents a share -- two months after projecting
it would turn a profit in the quarter.
Triumph Group (NYSE) rose 4.71, or 15%, to 35.85. The Wayne, Pa., aircraft-parts maker said fiscal
fourth-quarter profit rose, as it recorded a much smaller loss tied to the industrial gas turbine business, which
it exited earlier this year.
