Dow Industrials Rise 59.19 Points Ahead of Fed Meeting
Monday's Markets
Dow Industrials Rise 59.19 Points Ahead of Fed Meeting
By E.S. Browning
443 words
3 May 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS CHEWED their fingernails over oil prices and today's Federal Reserve meeting, but then settled
down and sent stocks higher at session's end.
Despite a sharp rise in oil prices and a widespread expectation that Fed policy makers will raise their base
interest rate another quarter percentage point to 3%, the Dow Jones Industrial Average finished ahead 59.19
points, or 0.58%, at 10251.70. It remains down 4.9% for 2005.
Oil prices fell to start the day, but oil had fallen so much in recent days that speculative buying developed in
the afternoon, pushing crude-oil futures back above $50 a barrel -- up $1.20 to $50.92 a barrel on the New
York Mercantile Exchange.
April manufacturing numbers were light, but March construction data were strong.
Based on a forecast of slowing profit growth, the global stock strategist at J.P. Morgan Securities advised
clients to cut stock holdings to 55% of portfolios, from 70%.
Investors now are waiting to see whether the Fed gives any hint today that it might change the pace of
interest-rate increases in the future. They also are looking ahead to Friday's report on April hiring, which could
offer hints on both economic growth and inflation. "People are undecided on what to do. They are waiting for
the Fed meeting and for the unemployment data on Friday," said Tim Heekin, director of trading at San
Francisco brokerage house Thomas Weisel Partners.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.46%, or 5.31 points, to 1162.16, leaving it down 4.1% this year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.36%, or 7 points, to 1928.65, down 11.3% for 2005.
Gold fell $5.50 to $429.50 an ounce, its biggest decline in more than a month, and the dollar rose.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.07%, or 0.12 point, to 167.17.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.56 billion shares traded, 2,128 stocks rose and 1,143 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 2/32, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing the
yield down to 4.196%. The 30-year bond rose 1/32 to yield 4.515%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 105.01 yen, up from 104.85, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2860 from $1.2867.

Boom Town: Drilling for Natural Gas Faces A Sizable Hurdle: Fort Worth --- The U.S.'s Largest Field Lies Under 1.6 Million People; Not...
Boom Town: Drilling for Natural Gas Faces A Sizable Hurdle: Fort Worth --- The U.S.'s Largest Field
Lies Under 1.6 Million People; Not Everyone Will Benefit --- Avoiding Baseball Diamonds
By Russell Gold
1,969 words
29 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FORT WORTH, Texas -- The Barnett Shale formation is a wedge of blackboard-colored rock measuring 400
feet thick in places. It's packed with 27 trillion cubic feet of natural gas made from organic material that
decomposed several million years ago, according to government scientists. That's enough to supply all
gas-heated homes in the U.S. for more than five years.
It's also directly underneath Fort Worth, a booming metropolitan area home to 1.6 million people.
Extracting this gas -- once thought to be permanently trapped in the rock -- is creating a peculiar set of
challenges for energy companies and for the consumers whose properties lie above it. Laverne Stanley, who
lives near downtown Fort Worth, stands to reap monthly checks because she owns rights to the gas under
her house. In nearby Haslet, Bob Lane, who doesn't own mineral rights, had to listen to the whine of a
175-foot-tall drilling rig at the end of his street all night. He won't be paid a penny.
Energy companies, for their part, are spending time wooing suburban communities and planning drilling
routes that avoid golf courses and baseball diamonds.
Urban drilling has never been undertaken on such a giant scale. Oil and gas wells still dot neighborhoods in
Oklahoma City and Los Angeles but those fields were discovered in the 1920s and developed when local
opposition was minimal. Drillers also left many oil-free neighborhoods untouched. By contrast, Fort Worth's
gassy rocks lie under the entire city and stretch underneath 16 counties. Any open field in the area is a
potential well site.
There are now 90 natural-gas drilling rigs poking holes in and around Fort Worth, more than one can find in
either Africa or Europe. The Barnett Shale, all by itself, is having a significant impact on the U.S. energy
sector. It's arresting steep declines in domestic gas production and tempering near-record prices for a fuel
critical for generating electricity and home heating.
At first, energy companies concentrated on a small section of the Barnett Shale about 30 miles north of
downtown. But the pace of drilling accelerated after word got out about good wells on the other side of the
city. That has created some new challenges.
It took 18 months of negotiations to hammer out plans to drill wells on the site of a proposed 8,900-home
community named Sendera Ranch. The property's developer, Centurion American Development Group,
originally wanted to build baseball diamonds on the four-acre well sites.
Devon Energy Corp. of Oklahoma City, which leased rights to drill on the property, objected. Devon knew it
would need to bring heavy equipment into the site every few years and officials worried that homeowners
would be furious when their baseball diamonds got trashed and backstops and dugouts removed. Instead,
Centurion plans to build soccer fields, which can more easily be disassembled and reconstructed. It will also
plant cedar trees with metal baskets around the roots that can be easily pulled up and put back in place.
For its part, Devon agreed to accelerate drilling so that rigs would be gone before home shoppers arrived.
The company also rerouted existing pipelines. "We didn't want someone digging in their backyard to build a
fence and hitting a pipeline," says David Keener, Centurion's chief financial officer.
The Woodhaven Country Club, an exclusive 18-hole golf course in Fort Worth, leased the right to drill under
its 150 acres. But the deal was structured to ensure the driller had no access to the club grounds. Duffers
would never know when a drill bit was chewing its way 7,000 feet underneath their feet.
With individual homeowners, the situation is more complicated. Property rights in Texas are split between
surface owners, who can build a house on the land, and mineral owners, who have rights to oil or gas
discovered underneath. Because few people thought there was much oil and gas near Fort Worth, mineral

--- Page 66 ---
Page 66 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and surface rights used to be neatly transferred from owner to owner. In the past couple of years, as word
spread, property owners have grown unwilling to part with their mineral rights, even if they sell their house.
Without mineral rights, land owners sitting on millions of dollars in natural gas aren't entitled to a dime.
"The word is out," says Dan Proctor, a Fort Worth developer. "There is no property you can buy, from here to
I-don't-know-where, that you can still buy the mineral rights."
One or two holdouts in a neighborhood won't prevent a well from being sunk. Under Texas regulations, a
Barnett Shale well -- and its associated drills and pipes -- must run at least 330 feet away from any unleased
property. Opposition from a larger number of people, however, can render a project unviable if a pipe had to
snake awkwardly through a neighborhood in order to remain on approved land.
Prospective drillers typically promise homeowners that they'll use a horizontal technique to hide unsightly
above-ground equipment. Horizontal wells go straight down before gradually bending and running parallel to
the surface.
Until a couple years ago, the idea of drilling in Fort Worth was farfetched. The first breakthrough came when
Mitchell Energy &Development Corp. figured out how to break open the Barnett Shale without breaking the
bank. The shale is incredibly dense and hard, tough enough that federal scientists working on a similar
formation in Colorado once tried, with mixed success, to see if they could break it open with a nuclear
explosion.
Shale is honeycombed with tiny pores that aren't well-connected. The natural gas is trapped in those pores
and has also been absorbed into the rock. The wells aim to drain the gas trapped in the pores and shake
loose the gas merged into the shale.
For years, Mitchell tried pumping an expensive and gooey chemical cocktail into the shale. Under
extraordinary pressure, the mixture would create tiny cracks in rock. But the chemical also gummed up the
pathways that allowed gas to flow.
In 1997, Mitchell's engineers tested a new "fracturing" technique using a mixture of water and sand. Fed by
several pumps on flatbed trucks, water is pumped in until it fills the confined space. Workers slowly increase
the pressure -- which can reach 3,500 pounds per square inch, roughly 100 times the pressure of a car tire --
until the rock cracks. The break can be half an inch wide and a few feet in length. The water and sand fill the
crack and build more pressure, until the crack elongates. The sand then prevents the rock from sliding back
into place after the water is drained, allowing gas to flow into the well.
It turned out the lighter "frac jobs" worked better than chemicals and were also cheaper. These days,
specialty oilfield service companies pump as much as seven million gallons of water per well -- enough to fill
the reflecting pool in front of Washington's Lincoln Memorial.
After its discovery, Mitchell Energy used the new technique and drilled hundreds of wells. In 2002, Devon
bought Mitchell for $3.2 billion, largely because of its gas prospects in the Barnett Shale area.
Because some homeowners living above the Barnett Shale retain mineral rights to their property, while others
don't, the gas-drilling boom has created a split between the haves and the have-nots.
"There are two kinds of surface owners: happy ones that own the mineral rights and unhappy ones that
don't," says Dick Lowe, a partner in Fort Worth's Four Sevens Oil Co. Four Sevens has drilled about 20 wells
in Fort Worth and has an additional 50 planned.
Ms. Stanley is one of the happy homeowners. She bought her $300,000 home east of downtown Fort Worth a
decade ago because she liked the towering oak trees and lakes. She had no idea that her home would end
up in the middle of one of the biggest natural-gas plays in North America. Four Sevens wooed her and her
neighbors. It held community meetings.
"We don't want to drill in your subdivision," said Four Sevens Chief Geologist Larry Brogdon, addressing Ms.
Stanley's community, Riverbend Estates. "We want to drill underneath your subdivision." At the end of the
meeting, half the residents lined up to sign notarized leases. Since then, almost all the 210 other
homeowners in the gated community have consented to drilling.
The closest well will be nearly a mile away in a flood plain, next to a lumberyard. Using horizontal drilling, the
well will drop straight down for two miles, then take a gradual turn and travel sideways until it reaches the
rock underneath the subdivision. If it strikes gas, all she and her neighbors will see are royalty checks. "No
one is going to get to be a millionaire, but the checks could be $100 to $200 a month and they will last longer
than anyone here will live," she says. The company says Ms. Stanley's expectations are accurate.

--- Page 67 ---
Page 67 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A couple of homeowners have yet to put their signatures on a lease. "If they don't sign, that's fine," says Ms.
Stanley. "It's just so much more for the rest of us."
Twenty miles to the north in the rapidly growing suburb of Haslet, Mr. Lane isn't so happy. When he bought
his home two years ago, he also didn't realize that his quiet patch of suburbia was about to be invaded by
drilling. Then one morning last month, a red and white drilling rig was erected at the end of his street by XTO
Energy Inc. of Fort Worth. The steel structure is so well lit at night that Mr. Lane's neighbors have started
calling it the Eiffel Tower. A droning metallic whine -- the sound of the machinery chewing through rock --
could be heard at all hours. "They can drill wherever they want. That really unnerves me," he says.
The company didn't need to ask permission and will never pay Mr. Lane royalties because he doesn't own the
mineral rights, which were retained by the property's previous owner. By local ordinance, the energy company
needed only to stay 400 feet away from the nearest home. The neighbors didn't even get a letter advising
them what was about to happen.
Municipal officials have a big incentive to allow drilling. Their coffers are being boosted by taxes on the value
of the underground gas, large signing bonuses and royalty checks for city-owned land. The Fort Worth city
council recently awarded rights to drill underneath city parks after a round of bidding. In early April, the city's
mayor was handed a check for $1.4 million from Dallas-based Dale Resources LLC, the signing bonus from
the first contract.
In Haslet, Mayor Francis Leong created a co-operative in the 800-acre Hugh White Estates subdivision where
he lives so that smaller landowners could also benefit. All the residents pooled their mineral rights, a move
that attracted Four Sevens, which was able to sign paperwork with a single entity instead of 200
homeowners. The royalties are divided up among the co-op members based on how many acres they own.
Mr. Leong owns a five-acre lot in the subdivision and was entitled to a 1/160th share.
Royalty checks are running about $250 per acre per month, Mr. Leong says. He's using his windfall to build a
weekend home on a nearby reservoir.

Carry Trade's Appeal Ebbs --- Investing Strategy Gets Riskier as Interest Rates, Inflation Climb
Going Global
Carry Trade's Appeal Ebbs --- Investing Strategy Gets Riskier as Interest Rates, Inflation Climb
By Michael R. Sesit
745 words
28 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Paris -- THE SO-CALLED carry trade has propelled a variety of risky markets to astronomical heights in
recent years. But many contend the viability of the strategy is coming to an end. That could lead to steep
declines in the very same markets that the carry trade once buoyed.
Simply put, the carry trade involves borrowing money at low interest rates and investing it for a higher return,
a strategy that every bank in the world pursues, often involving Treasury securities. In its more sophisticated
forms, hedge funds, banks and other speculators used cheap dollars to finance investments in riskier assets
-- junk bonds, emerging-market stocks and bonds, mortgage securities, commodities and stocks generally.
Even individuals -- especially in the U.S. -- got into the act, borrowing low-cost dollars to finance everything
from a new house to investments in markets they couldn't locate on a map and companies whose names they
couldn't pronounce.
The popularity of the strategy is partly responsible for the outsize gains in recent years in many markets.
From their postbubble lows of late September 2001 to their highs of early March 2005, global emerging
markets rose 139%, measured in dollars, according to Morgan Stanley Capital International. Among the stars:
Hungary, which soared 436%, and the Czech Republic, up 545%, both in dollars.
By contrast, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 33% in that period. Riskier bonds also were a popular
bet, driving the gap in yields of emerging-market bonds over U.S. Treasurys down to just 3.25 percentage
points in March from 9.30 percentage points in September 2001, according to J.P. Morgan. Meanwhile, easy
money sent commodity prices soaring and those for property in the U.S., Britain, Australia, Spain, France and
Ireland through the roof.
But that was yesterday. As U.S. interest rates rise and inflation starts to pick up, the strategy no longer
looks so attractive and many are rushing to unwind their positions by selling riskier assets to pay back their
borrowed funds.
The carry trade "has been the trade of the last two years; everybody's been leveraging up because of cheap
money in the U.S.," says Daniel Birch, a strategist at London-based brokers Execution Ltd. "But now that the
cheap money is disappearing, these trades have become riskier. People who borrowed dollars now run the
risk of losing money."
Rising U.S. rates will raise the cost of capital in the U.S., cause U.S. household assets to decline in value and
prompt the heavily indebted American consumer to start saving and stop spending, predicts David Roche,
president of investment consultants Independent Strategy. "Global growth and financial markets will not go
unscathed, even if the epicenter of the damage will be in the U.S.," he adds.
Cracks are already appearing as investors become more risk-averse: Emerging-market and junk-bond
spreads are widening; volatility is increasing; emerging stock markets have fallen 7.9% since the end of
February; U.K. house prices have fallen in some months.
"Investors are getting less and less compensation for taking risks," says Rolf Elgeti, head of European equity
strategy at ABN Amro Bank. He likens the U.S. Federal Reserve's steady dose of quarter-point interest-rate
increases to "a slow death" for the carry trade.
Investors may not be able to hide from the fallout from a collapsing carry trade, but they can lessen the pain.
Independent Strategy's Mr. Roche advises shorting -- betting on a price fall -- poor-quality emerging market
bonds, industrial commodities and financial, housing and consumer durable stocks in "all Anglo-Saxon"
markets and several others, such as Spain and France.

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For investors who can't or don't want to go short, he says buying Asian currencies, euros, euro-zone and
Japanese equities and euro-area bonds "will save the remains of the day," he says. The best insurance
against widespread weakness in the financial sector, he adds, is gold.
Last week, ABN recommended that investors underweight banks, which are exposed to the carry trade both
as practitioners and lenders to others who are exposed. The Dutch bank also warned that companies that sell
heavily in emerging markets -- such as Spanish utilities, phone companies and banks -- are vulnerable. Ditto
for lower-rated companies.

Abreast of the Market
Verizon, Boeing Help Stocks Climb --- Major Indexes Post Gains On a Drop in Price of Oil; Amazon
Data Disappoint
By Karen Talley Dow Jones Newswires
902 words
28 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks rose as the price of oil fell almost 5%, but the Dow Jones Industrial Average was also assisted by
Verizon Communications and Boeing, while the Nasdaq Composite Index gained despite disappointing
earnings from Amazon.com.
The Dow gained 47.67 points, or 0.47%, to 10198.80, the Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 2.99, or
0.16%, to 1930.43, and the S&P 500 added 4.64, or 0.4%, to 1156.38.
"Oil was clearly the main catalyst for the market's turnaround," said Marc Pado, U.S. market strategist at
Cantor Fitzgerald. "But the Commerce Department [durable-goods] report allowed people to invest with more
confidence as the day went on because it showed that growth in the economy is clearly slowing and interest
rates will stay under control."
There was also an upbeat earnings report from Verizon Communications, which ended up as the Dow's best
percentage performer. Shares advanced $1.22, or 3.6%, to $35.22 after the nation's largest local phone
carrier said first-quarter profit rose 47%, helped by lower pension-related expenses and by strong gains in
wireless and high-speed Internet service.
Boeing gained 66 cents, or 1.1%, to 59.66. The aerospace giant reported a drop in first-quarter profit, though
it topped analysts' estimates and held to its forecast for commercial airplane deliveries in 2005 and 2006.
Amazon.com (Nasdaq) lost 99 cents, or 3%, to 31.72. The online shopping empire's aggressive use of
discounted shipping to draw in customers helped boost its quarterly revenue but contributed to a 30% decline
in profit.
InfoSpace (Nasdaq) lost more than one-quarter of its market value, plunging 12.29, or 27%, to 32.70.
First-quarter earnings rose, but the Web search and mobile-content delivery company issued a
second-quarter outlook for revenue and earnings that was below analysts' expectations.
Tupperware rose 1.83, or 9.2%, to 21.63. The maker of plastic containers reported higher sales and earnings
in the first quarter and boosted its outlook for the full year.
Corning gained 1.01, or 8%, to 13.57. Spurred by higher demand for fiber-optic equipment, the company
reported first-quarter earnings more than quadrupled.
Corinthian Colleges (Nasdaq) fell 2.85, or 17%, to 14.02. The higher-education provider said fiscal
fourth-quarter earnings will be below Wall Street's expectations because of sluggish growth in enrollments.
WellPoint gained 6.42, or 5.4%, to 124.45. The health-benefits provider said first-quarter profit more than
doubled amid medical-enrollment growth from its November combination with Anthem.
Phelps Dodge lost 3.70, or 4.2%, to 85.54. The world's largest publicly traded copper producer saw
first-quarter earnings more than double, but it cited higher production costs attributable, in part, to steeper
energy and freight costs.
Aflac rose 3.48, or 9.6%, to 39.63. The disability insurer's first-quarter profit rose, topping expectations, as
higher sales in its key market of Japan offset a decline in U.S. sales.
C.H. Robinson Worldwide (Nasdaq) rose 3.52, or 7.4%, to 50.82. The transportation-services provider posted
first-quarter per-share earnings of 48 cents, up from 34 cents a year ago.

Allergan lost 2.69, or 3.7%, to 70.32. The Botox maker posted first-quarter per-share earnings that were flat
with those from a year ago.

a 2-for-1 common stock split.

Finding Someone to Fill Greenspan's Shoes --- Feldstein, Bernanke and Hubbard Are Seen as Front-Runners to Succeed the Fed Chairman
Politics &Policy
Finding Someone to Fill Greenspan's Shoes --- Feldstein, Bernanke and Hubbard Are Seen as
Front-Runners to Succeed the Fed Chairman
By Greg Ip
1,344 words
27 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SOMETIME IN THE NEXT nine months, President Bush will make one of the most important appointments of
his presidency, one that history suggests will affect the economy in the U.S. and abroad long past the end of
his term: Selecting a successor to Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan.
Other candidates could yet emerge, but for now there appear to be three front-runners, all with sterling
academic pedigrees: Martin Feldstein, 65 years old, a Harvard University economist who advised President
Reagan and has been a leading advocate of Social Security private accounts; R. Glenn Hubbard, 46, who
served in the Bush White House and now is dean of Columbia University's graduate business school, and
Ben Bernanke, 51, a Fed governor who has been nominated to be chairman of the Council of Economic
Advisers, a White House post previously held by the other two men and by Mr. Greenspan.
With Mr. Greenspan due to step down in January from the post he has held since 1987, the White House will
likely seek a successor who reassures the markets while being philosophically attuned to Mr. Bush's overall
economic agenda. The chief job of the Fed chairman is to set interest rates, but -- especially under Mr.
Greenspan -- the Fed chairman also has emerged as the arbiter of sound fiscal policy, and Mr. Bush is likely
to steer clear of someone he thinks would criticize his budget policies.
Both Mr. Feldstein and Mr. Hubbard helped shape Mr. Bush's priorities of cutting taxes and creating private
accounts as part of Social Security. That doesn't mean they will hold down interest rates to boost Mr. Bush's
popularity. Like Mr. Bernanke, both have vigorously asserted the importance of the Fed's keeping inflation
close to zero.
"Every president wants and chooses someone who he believes will not harm him," says Allan Meltzer, a
Carnegie Mellon University economist and expert on Fed history. Once appointed, he notes, chairmen often
then assert their independence.
The White House hasn't started the formal search for his successor. It's "premature," White House Deputy
Chief of Staff Karl Rove said last week. "It's important that the Fed chairman remain strong throughout his
entire term."
Outsiders note that by January a better candidate may emerge or economic circumstances might call for
someone with different strengths.
Of the three men assumed to be the leading contenders, Mr. Feldstein may have the edge in economic
stature and political experience. As chairman of President Reagan's Council of Economic Advisers, he called
for spending cuts and tax increases to reduce the budget deficit. That angered some White House officials
but enhanced his public reputation as "someone who was forthright" on fiscal policy, says Stanford University
economist Robert Hall, a longtime associate. Mr. Feldstein has been advising Mr. Bush since 1999.
Mr. Feldstein's past deficit concerns have some conservatives worrying that as Fed chairman, he would
criticize Mr. Bush's fiscal policies, but that seems unlikely. "That was 20 years ago," says Cesar Conda,
formerly an aide to Vice President Dick Cheney and now a partner in Navigators LLC, a lobbying firm. "When
I was at the White House, he was a solid, free-market economist, always on the side of the president's
tax-cutting agenda while there were naysayers in the press and on the Hill concerned about the deficit."
In an interview, Mr. Feldstein says the deficit is much smaller today as a share of gross domestic product
than it was in the early 1980s, and it will likely decline further even if the tax cuts are made permanent,
provided the administration is "even reasonably successful" in controlling discretionary spending.
Mr. Feldstein also has provided intellectual heft to the Social Security private-accounts effort, though some
admirers criticize him for playing down the scale of the benefit cuts or tax increases needed to restore the

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
program's solvency. Though monetary policy isn't one of Mr. Feldstein's academic specialties, he has
written that the economy benefits from an inflation rate close to zero.
One area of possible embarrassment for Mr. Feldstein involves his service on the board of American
International Group Inc., the New York insurer now under investigation. Mr. Feldstein says he won't discuss
the companies where he serves on the board; he says regulators haven't questioned him about AIG.
Regulators say AIG's board has moved quickly to address their concerns.
Mr. Hubbard, as chairman of Mr. Bush's Council of Economic Advisers from 2001 to 2003, won admiration for
his discipline and work ethic on issues ranging from the West Coast dockworker lockout to the 2003
dividend-tax cut. He was known for getting his staff to quickly draft authoritative policy memos for White
House decision makers. "He earned his way into the Oval Office," says a former administration official.
Mr. Hubbard was a vigorous promoter of the 2003 Bush tax cuts, in particular on corporate dividends, which
he helped shape. Behind the scenes, he quarreled with Mr. Greenspan over the Fed chairman's view that the
resulting deficits would push up interest rates. These stands endeared Mr. Hubbard to conservatives but
aroused suspicions among some that he is highly political. He has since co-written a lengthy academic study
arguing that the link between deficits and interest rates is hard to pin down.
Mr. Hubbard has written extensively on the interplay between the Fed and the markets. In a January column
in BusinessWeek he said the Fed, "wisely insulated from the political process," deserved a "gold medal" for
achieving price stability.
Mr. Bernanke conducted research on monetary policy at Princeton University before joining the Fed in
2002. Since then, his plain-speaking style, numerous speeches and copious research have made him second
in prominence at the Fed only to Mr. Greenspan. He recently said the hardest part of moving to the Fed was
having to wear a suit: "My proposal that Fed governors should signal their commitment to public service by
wearing Hawaiian shirts and Bermuda shorts has so far gone unheeded."
He may need to pay more political dues before getting a shot at the chairmanship. Though a libertarian
Republican, he displays few partisan leanings. In 2000, as chairman of Princeton's economics department, he
hired star economist Paul Krugman, now a vociferous Bush critic, from Massachusetts Institute of
Technology. Mr. Bush will get a better feel for Mr. Bernanke's views during his stint at the Council of
Economic Advisers, though people close to the process say the post isn't an audition for the Fed job.
There could be several dark-horse candidates. Donald Kohn, a longtime Fed staffer and now governor, is
well placed to emulate Mr. Greenspan but he is a political independent. Consultant Lawrence Lindsey has
close ties to the White House and was a Fed governor, but his record as Mr. Bush's first National Economic
Council director was mixed. John Taylor is a prominent monetary-policy scholar but he didn't leave a big
mark in the four years he just spent as Treasury's top international official.
---
John Harwood contributed to this article.

Dow Industrials Rally by 2.06% On Profit News --- Broader Markets Also Rebound On Signs of Economic Strength; Bond Prices Decline Sharply
Thursday's Markets
Dow Industrials Rally by 2.06% On Profit News --- Broader Markets Also Rebound On Signs of
Economic Strength; Bond Prices Decline Sharply
By E.S. Browning
725 words
22 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER FALLING MORE than 900 points over seven weeks, the Dow Jones Industrial Average rebounded
206.24 points in a day, its biggest one-day gain since 2003.
After threatening Wednesday to fall below 10000 for the first time since October, blue-chip shares rebounded
2.06% to 10218.60, amid continuing upbeat profit news and signs of economic strength. Money raced to
stocks from the relative safety of bonds and cash, sending most indexes up sharply and sending bond prices
down heavily.
Within hours, the debate on Wall Street changed from how low stocks would go, to whether the rally was a
one-day wonder or something sustainable.
"There has been so much volatility in the past week or so that I don't feel very comfortable calling for anything
but more volatility," said Brian Pears, head stock trader at Victory Capital Management, the
money-management arm of KeyCorp in Cleveland.
One worry for market veterans was that past periods of market decline have seen similar one-day jumps,
which proved difficult to sustain.
This time, the Dow industrials had posted four 100-point declines in six sessions and were overdue for a
bounce, traders said. Investors were delaying stock purchases as stocks swooned, "and the valuations just
got to points where they found stocks to be cheap," said Bob Basel, senior trader at Citigroup's brokerage
arm in New York. "The question is whether we can sustain this for more than one day."
One hopeful sign came after regular trading hours, as Google reported far better quarterly profit than
expected. That stock rose 9.2% in after-hours trading, raising hopes for at least a temporary extension to
yesterday's gains.
The immediate catalyst for yesterday's gains was strong quarterly profit news from companies including
cellular-phone makers Motorola and Nokia. On top of that came a strong weekly jobs report, indicating that
new jobless claims fell to a 10-week low last week.
Then, at midday in New York, as investors were trying to decide how sustainable the rebound was going to
be, a survey by the Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia was released, showing strong business activity in
that region. That eased recent fears of an economic slowdown, and helped add an extra 100 points to the
day's stock gains.
In addition, Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan, in Senate testimony, belittled fears that the economy might be
faced with "stagflation," the combination of inflation and weak growth that hobbled the economy in the
1970s.
Beneath the surface, worries remained that persistent inflation could force the Fed to keep raising interest
rates for much of this year, stifling economic growth. However, quarterly profits, which had been expected to
rise about 8%, are coming in closer to 11%, helping create some optimism among investors. Whether
investors will persist in their fears, or finally be won over by the stronger-than-expected profit reports, could
determine the market's longer-term direction.
In heavy trading, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index advanced 1.97%, or 22.45 points, to 1159.95, still
down 4.3% for 2005. Its point and percentage gains were the largest since 2003.
The Nasdaq Composite Index jumped 2.54%, or 48.65 points, to 1962.41, down 9.8% this year. Its gains
were the largest in more than a year.

Blue Chips Fall Close to 10000, Finish off 1.14% --- Upbeat Profit Announcements Can't Outweigh Inflation News; Fifth Decline in Six...

Blue Chips Fall Close to 10000, Finish off 1.14% --- Upbeat Profit Announcements Can't Outweigh
Inflation News; Fifth Decline in Six Sessions
By E.S. Browning
656 words
21 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES INDUSTRIAL Average fell almost to 10000 and then bounced back, still finishing at its
lowest level in nearly six months.
Upbeat profit announcements couldn't outweigh bad inflation news contained in both the March
consumer-price report and the Federal Reserve's "beige book" report on the economy.
Dropping as low as 10000.46 shortly before regular trading ended, the Dow industrials finished at 10012.36,
off 115.05 points, or 1.14%. The index is down 7.15% for 2005. It was the fifth decline in six sessions, and
four of them have been of more than 100 points. Other indexes also fell sharply.
Stocks began the day with gains on the back of positive quarterly earnings reports from Intel, Caterpillar and
United Technologies, all components of the Dow. But selling overwhelmed the market as the day went on.
Part of the problem was the inflation news, which reinforced fears that the Fed may have to accelerate its
interest-rate increases to cool the economy. Part was simply a concern among short-term investors that an
uncertain economic outlook is sending stocks down, and that there is no money to be made by standing in
the way.
"Buyers seemed just to pull away. They were willing to let stocks drop," said stock trader Michael Driscoll at
New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns. "There was no one who was going to be heroic enough to stand here
and buy."
The Wall Street jitters could be seen in a sharp increase in demand for options, often used as a hedge
against falling stock prices. A widely watched measure of option-related fear, the Chicago Board Options
Exchange's volatility index, or VIX, jumped 13%. It was up 22% Friday, another day of sharp stock selling.
Intel, Caterpillar and UT were the only members of the 30-component Dow industrials to manage a gain. The
three also boosted forecasts for future performance.
Reflecting the day, gold rose for a fourth consecutive session, hitting $435.30 an ounce, its highest finish in
almost a month.
Consumer prices rose more than economists expected in March, even when oil prices were stripped out. As
investors were trying to decide what to do, the Fed's beige book was released in the afternoon, warning that
its economists also were seeing signs of higher prices. The stock declines worsened after that.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 1.33%, or 15.28 points, to 1137.50, down 6% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.96%, or 18.60 points, to 1913.76. The S&P 500 is at its lowest level since
November and the Nasdaq is almost back to its low for the year, hit last week.
The Dow industrials have been unable to stay lastingly above 10000 since first crossing that mark in March
1999. They have fallen back and finished below that level 24 times since then, and are threatening to do it for
a 25th time. They last finished below 10000 Oct. 26.
Outside the U.S., stocks were almost unchanged in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index,
excluding U.S. stocks, rose 0.01 point, to 168.49.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.77 billion shares traded, 847 stocks rose and 2,470 fell.

Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 3/32, or 93.75 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing the

The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 106.78 yen, up from 106.76, while the euro rose against

By E.S. Browning

The Wall Street Journal

English

UPBEAT CORPORATE-PROFIT news and benign inflation figures fueled cautious optimism on Wall Street.
After its worst weekly decline in two years, the Dow Jones Industrial Average bounced back 56.16 points, or
0.56%, to 10127.41, on positive earnings news from companies including Coca-Cola and Texas Instruments.
Today, stocks could benefit from Intel's strong profit report, announced after regular hours. In after-hours
trading, Intel rose to $23.33, according to Inet ATS Inc.
March wholesale-inflation numbers, excluding energy and food, barely rose, assuaging fears that oil might
push up prices elsewhere. Consumer-price figures are due today.
"It is going to be choppy for the next couple of months, but I think the market is going to bounce back," said
money manager H. Giles Knight of Gartmore Global Investments in West Conshohocken, Pa.
Bond prices surged on the inflation news, driving yields down. William Poole, president of the St. Louis
Federal Reserve Bank, called the inflation outlook "very favorable," spreading hope that the Fed will avoid
sharp interest-rate increases.
But the stock rebound was limited by the sharpest rise in crude-oil futures since February, a gain of $1.92 to
$52.29 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, which was blamed on refinery problems. Gold hit a
one-month high and the dollar fell. Some investors call the stock recovery fragile and dependent on future
economic and profit news.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.59%, or 6.80, to 1152.78. The Nasdaq Composite Index gained
1.02%, or 19.44 points, to 1932.36.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose
1.21%, or 2.01 points, to 168.48.

down to 4.191%. The 30-year bond was up 1 5/32 to yield 4.531%.

Is Something Wrong?
Is Something Wrong?
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,341 words
19 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections
Brian S. Wesbury's editorial-page commentary yesterday erred in stating that the House voted in favor of a
Chinese import-tariff bill. It was, in fact, the Senate.
(WSJ April 20, 2005)
(END)
Just six weeks ago, the Dow Jones Industrial Average was trading at a four-year high, less than 7% below its
all-time peak. Last Friday, the Dow had its worst day in two years when every major equity index fell to new
lows for the year. In the past six weeks, the Dow has fallen 7.9%, the S&P 500 by 6.5% and Nasdaq by 8.5%.
These declines have spooked investors and earned financial markets a spot on the evening news. Blame for
the slide has been placed on a combination of earnings warnings, weak economic data, Fed rate hikes, rising
oil prices, and an expanding trade deficit.
Paul Krugman, a New York Times columnist, wrote yesterday about "a whiff of stagflation," reminiscent of the
'70s. A week ago, Paul Volcker, former Fed chairman, wrote about "huge imbalances, disequilibria, [and]
risks" that have created circumstances "as dangerous and intractable" as he can remember. These are not
isolated thoughts. Many fret about these issues. So is something terribly wrong? Is the economy really in
trouble? A perusal of general economic data suggests that the answer is no.
The 2003 tax cut, which reduced tax rates on capital to the lowest level in decades, continues to fuel
economic activity as evidenced by the surge in business investment, profit growth and dividends. The real
fed-funds rate remains near zero, even after seven "measured" Fed rate hikes. Corporate and household
balance sheets are in good shape, and entrepreneurial activity is vibrant.
While retail sales were weaker than expected in March, they are a volatile data series that has disappointed
on at least five different monthly occasions in the past two years. Removing the month-to-month volatility,
non-auto retail sales are up 7.5% at an annual rate in the past six months -- a very strong showing.
Markets are rarely surprised by shifting economic tides on such short notice. Policy shifts, on the other hand,
are a different story. For example, following the election in 2004, markets got a nice boost as the odds of
pro-growth fiscal policy increased dramatically. But this boost is evaporating as it now appears that fiscal
policy is adrift. While the House of Representatives voted to eliminate the estate tax last week, it offset this
potential positive in the previous week by voting to slap a tariff of 27.5% on all Chinese imports. And in the
Senate, a train wreck is looming.
Democrats have filibustered many of the president's judicial nominees and are now using other procedural
methods to block his picks to head the FDA and EPA. Republicans are frustrated by this, and are
contemplating a "nuclear option." The Democrats have threatened to retaliate. No matter what your view is of
these developments, gridlock could easily worsen. And while gridlock is typically a good thing in Washington,
there is potential that partisan wrangling could hold up progress on dealing with long-term issues. Momentum
toward personal accounts in Social Security has seemingly stalled, as has any vote on the extension of the
2003 tax cuts. Energy policy, even with $50-oil and $2.50-gasoline, remains moribund.
In discussions about the fate of the stock market in recent days, very little has been said about developments
in the nation's capital. Despite clear historical evidence that shows how important fiscal policy decisions are to
financial markets, economists, strategists and analysts consistently overlook these developments. For
example, the stagflation that worries Mr. Krugman does not appear out of thin air, it takes years of bad policy
to create. Rising oil prices and one month's slightly weaker-than-expected retail sales data do not qualify as
stagflation.

--- Page 89 ---
Page 89 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stagflation is not easily created. It is a task for real professionals. The seeds of the '70s stagflation were
planted in the mid-'60s. Great Society spending, and the high taxes needed to pay for it, held back the
economy. At the same time, the Fed kept interest rates low in an attempt to overcome the burdens of a
growing government sector. By the early '70s, the pressures were so intense that President Nixon closed the
gold window and used wage and price controls in an attempt to hold back inflation.
What was most interesting about that time period from an investors' point of view is that the equity market
started to show weakness as early as late 1965, well before any actual experience with stagflation began.
The market recognized the potential for economic problems well before they occurred.
The question for today is whether or not the market is beginning to price in any damage that could result from
current policy. Federal government spending has increased 34.7% in the past five years, the Medicare drug
benefit added an estimated unfunded liability of $18.2 trillion to long-term entitlement costs, Sarbanes-Oxley
has increased both the costs and risks associated with business decisions, and important fiscal policy
reforms are caught up in partisan wrangling.
At the same time, the Fed is encountering a great deal of resistance in its rate-hiking campaign. And this is
occurring despite the fact that interest rates are very low in both real and nominal terms. Between 1995 and
1999, when the S&P 500 was rising at an average annual rate of 25.7%, the fed-funds rate averaged 5.4%,
nearly twice as high as today's 2.75%. The real rate in the late '90s averaged 3.0%, while today it is virtually
zero. Moreover, inflation was declining in the late '90s, while it is on the rise this year.
Easy Fed policy is boosting aggregate demand and nominal GDP growth has accelerated from 3.2% in 2001
to 6.6% in the past year, driving inflation up as well. On the supply side, a wave of technology investment
has continued to lift productivity at near record historical levels. However, every day that government burdens
grow is a day closer to the time when efficiency gains begin to falter as investment moves to other areas of
the global economy.
But none of this has to happen, and there is still a high probability that it will not. The current environment is
best compared to the early '80s, not the mid-'60s or '70s. In the early '80s, fears such as those recently
expressed by Mr. Volcker were widespread. But the consistent move by the U.S. toward global freedom and
pro-growth policies at home eventually won the day. Interestingly, by fighting inflation, Mr. Volcker was part
of the solution to stagflation in the early '80s.
While it may seem like the end of the world to some, the Dow is still up 38.4% from its low point in 2002. To
keep it moving forward, however, the White House must re-engage on the policy front and the Fed must
continue to lift rates. In addition, while momentum has slowed in the move toward Social Security reform,
once the legislation has been drafted and formally introduced into Congress, demagoguery will be much more
difficult to accomplish.
Under the leadership of Connie Mack and John Breaux, the President's Tax Reform Panel is doing good
work. A compelling case for tax reform, built over many months of public testimony, will be hard for Congress
to ignore, no matter how partisan the infighting has become.
In the end, it is policies that matter, especially when thousands of hedge funds are willing to make decisions
immediately when they see the direction of political winds changing. Fiscal policy which encourages
entrepreneurial activity, and monetary policy which pulls the reins on inflation, are best for long-term gains
in economic activity, jobs, incomes, and equity values.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief investment strategist at Claymore Advisors, LLC.

19 April 2005

Spring Home Sales Start Strong --- Key Forecast Is Revised Upward, Despite Pockets Of Weakness; Rebounds in the O.C. and Boston
Spring Home Sales Start Strong --- Key Forecast Is Revised Upward, Despite Pockets Of Weakness;
Rebounds in the O.C. and Boston
By Ruth Simon
1,697 words
19 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER A MULTIYEAR real-estate boom that has raised concerns about a housing bubble in a number of
markets, economists and homeowners are closely watching this year's spring selling season for any signs of
a slowdown.
So far, there aren't many of them.
Spring tends to be the hottest selling season, with families looking to move well before the start of the next
school year. And sales are getting off to a strong start in many parts of the country.
In a revised forecast, the National Association of Realtors now says it expects sales of existing single-family
homes to fall 2.4% to 6.62 million for the full year, which still would be the second-highest level on record.
That is better than the 5% slide the Realtors group predicted at the start of the year.
One reason: The much-anticipated increase in long-term mortgage rates has yet to materialize. Weak
economic news has helped push rates on 30-year fixed-rate mortgages below 6%, from a recent high of
6.17%, according to HSH Associates, providing a nudge to buyers fearful that rates will shoot up again.
The brisk start to the season comes as concerns about the housing market have been escalating. In a
speech yesterday, Federal Reserve Governor Susan Bies said, "We are beginning to see signs that housing
prices may be reaching a peak in some markets." She cited the growing share of homes purchased by
investors, and the large number of borrowers using adjustable-rate, interest-only mortgages in an effort to
make high-priced homes more affordable.
Additionally, there is evidence of a weakening in certain corners of the market. In the luxury market, for
instance, "what we're finding is that properties that sold . . . in the first quarter of 2005 seem to be on the
market longer" in a number of locales, says Stuart Siegel, president of Sotheby's International Realty, a unit
of Cendant Corp., blaming the "net impact of sticker shock."
There were signs last fall that the housing market, at long last, was beginning to soften in such places as
Orange County, Calif., and Boston. Real-estate brokers say business picked up in many markets after a brief
slowdown, helped along by declining mortgage rates. In some local markets, improving economic conditions
have played a role. In Atlanta, corporate relocations plus a sense of increased job security have helped boost
sales of homes priced at more than $1 million, says Chris Ballard, broker-owner of Century 21 Gold Medal
Realty.
In some markets, such as Phoenix and Newport News, Va., real-estate brokers say sales are stronger this
year than last. In some cases, homes are flying off the market within days or even hours of being listed.
Brokers in some markets say the rise in sales that normally arrives with spring began earlier this year.
The likelihood of yet another frenzied spring comes on top of record sales last year. Fueled by low mortgage
rates, sales of existing homes hit 6.78 million, topping a record of 6.18 million in 2003, according to the
National Association of Realtors. Home prices climbed more than 20% last year in Miami, Las Vegas,
Phoenix, Los Angeles and Washington, D.C., among other markets.
Economists generally agree that price gains will slow to more normal levels, though just when this will happen
-- and how deep the slowdown will be -- remains an open question. Home-price research company Fiserv
CSW Inc. expects some slowing this year. It forecasts that home prices will rise an average of 7% to 9% this
year, following last year's 14.3% rise, in the 90 major metropolitan areas it tracks.
NAR chief economist David Lereah expects a slowdown in the second half. So far this year, he says, the
number of homes sold is running about flat compared with last year's strong showing.

--- Page 93 ---
Page 93 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The strong housing market "will continue as long as interest rates remain relatively low and credit remains
relatively easy," says Kenneth Rosen, chairman of the Fisher Center for Real Estate and Urban Economics at
the University of California at Berkeley. But, he adds, "the longer the boom goes on, the bigger the correction
will be."
In Newport News, Va., "it's even more frenzied than it was last year," says Liz Moore, president of Liz Moore
&Associates, where sales rose 43% in the first quarter from the year-earlier period. "If we have a reasonable
listing, we have multiple offers in the first couple of days -- five to seven isn't unusual," she says.
The supply of properties for sale is so thin in Manhattan, meanwhile, that more buyers this year are offering
$100,000 or more above the listed price.
Arlyne Blitz, a vice president with Corcoran Group, says one of her clients recently made an all-cash offer that
was $155,000 over the asking price for a two-bedroom, 1,500 square-foot condo priced at $1.35 million in an
arrangement that required would-be buyers to submit one "best and final" offer.
"We were No. 7 of 17," Ms. Blitz says. "The winning bid was for over $1.6 million." Some brokers there are
deliberately underpricing property to elicit greater bidding and a higher final selling price, she notes.
Properties also are flying off the market in Phoenix and Tucson, where an influx of refugees from Southern
California and Las Vegas -- looking for lower-cost housing or better investment opportunities -- is helping to
propel prices skyward. Last year, the average home in the Phoenix area stayed on the market for 50 to 60
days, says Bill Jilbert, president of Coldwell Banker Success Realty. Now, he says, "the pendulum is so far to
one side that it's not even fun."
Broker Tom Weiskopf put his own 3,000-square-foot four-bedroom home in Scottsdale, Ariz., on the market in
late February. Within six hours he had eight offers, with most buyers waiving the standard contingencies for
financing, appraisal and inspection.
Mr. Weiskopf cut off the bidding at 7 p.m. and accepted an offer for $645,500, $4,500 above the listed price.
The competition among buyers is especially fierce for entry-level homes. In Long Beach, Calif., "anything
under $500,000 in a decent neighborhood is going to produce multiple offers," says Richard F. Gaylord, a
broker with Re/Max Real Estate Specialists. Mr. Gaylord last month received 29 offers on a
1,084-square-foot, three-bedroom starter home that sold for $510,000, $60,500 more than the asking price.
Russell Garron, a pharmaceuticals salesman, initially offered $455,000 this month for a 1,200-square foot,
three-bedroom condo in Irvine, Calif., listed at $449,000.
Mr. Garron upped his offer to $465,000 because there were multiple bids. When his broker, Tamzi
Richardson of First Team Real Estate, called at 10:30 p.m. that night with the seller's response, Mr. Garron
immediately dashed to Kinko's to fax in his paperwork. While searching for a home, "I slept with my
cellphone," says Mr. Garron, who lost out on two other properties.
Not every market is as heated. In Kansas City, the average time on the market climbed to 57 days in the first
quarter from 49 days during the same period last year.
"We're not seeing the rush of multiple offers . . . [or] sales coming in well over the list price like we were
seeing last year or the year before," says Jerry Reece, president of Reece &Nichols there.
In the bellwether market of San Diego, home prices rose at an annualized rate of 12.5% in March, down from
as much as 26.4% in October, according to DataQuick Information Systems, which tracks U.S. real-estate
sales.
Houston broker Julius F. Zatopek III, of Re/Max on the Brazos, says sales have softened in that city in recent
weeks after spiking during the first quarter as buyers dived into the market, nervous about rising interest
rates.
In Miami, where sales remain strong, resale prices of existing condos in the $500,000 to $900,000 price
range could fall by as much as $200,000 later this year, predicts broker Mark Zilbert. With so many new units
coming onto the market, "the motivation is to be in a hip new property," Mr. Zilbert says. With existing units,
"the value isn't there."

say sales are actually stronger this year than last.
-- Brokers in other places say the spring selling season began earlier
this year.

Review / TV: Between God and Satan
Review / TV:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Review / TV: Between God and Satan
By Nancy deWolfe Smith
1,274 words
15 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W12
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
REVELATIONS," an eerie six-part NBC drama that began this week, was in the works long before last
November's election, so it's difficult to accuse the network of a knee-jerk attempt to play to the Red-State,
putatively fundamentalist-Christian masses out there. (Wednesdays, 9-10 p.m. ET.) The series -- in which a
devout nun and a skeptical scientist search for Christ in an effort to avert the Apocalypse -- does ask that its
audience take a leap of faith. Scriptural literalists (including some Muslims) will have to accept its premise
that human beings could actually intervene to forestall the preordained outcome of the final battle between
Jesus and Satan. Other viewers will need to suspend disbelief in divine signs and miracles.
That said, the situational setting for "Revelations" is something even an agnostic can relate to. These are
indeed troubled times, even if the troubles, such as war, are indubitably man-made. And what could be more
apocalyptic than the talk of global warming?
When the series opened Wednesday, we met Harvard astrophysicist Richard Massey (Bill Pullman) as he
returned a shattered man from Chile, where his 12-year-old daughter had her heart ripped out by a Satanic
cult leader. The killer, chillingly played by Michael Massee, has been extradited and imprisoned in the U.S.,
but you get the feeling he won't be there long. Meanwhile, Sister Josepha Montifiore (Natascha McElhone)
has been in Mexico, exploring an apparition -- the shadow of the cross on a mountainside -- for the
foundation that employs her.
Back in Miami, though, a more urgent drama is taking place. A lightning-struck young girl lies in a hospital
bed in a persistent vegetative state, and doctors are begging her father to pull the plug so they can harvest
her organs and save the life of another child. But a hospital priest summons Sister Montifiore because the
comatose girl has begun speaking, in Latin. Ultimately she directs Sister Montifiore to Dr. Massey in Boston,
with a drawing that seems to have come from his dead daughter.
As bizarre as that sounds, the show gets stranger still. The nun obtains a court order to deter the medical
authorities who want to pull the plug on the comatose girl in the hospital, and manages to transfer the patient
to a safe haven in a local convent. In view of recent events in the real world, that TV twist is now more
difficult to believe in than the biblical prediction of the End of Days.
There is no way the show's writers could have predicted the ordeal of Terri Schiavo -- for a pilot shot a year
ago. But no special insight is required for viewers to guess that our nun and scientist are destined to team up.
And so they do this week, setting off like some Mulder and Scully on a journey that will take them first to a
Greek island and ultimately to the Holy Land itself.
The presence of two movie stars and the high-quality production values make "Revelations" entertaining in its
own right. As for its religion theme, remember that the HBO series "Carnivale" has been exploring a similar
apocalyptic clash (between a man with healing powers and a Satanic figure who's got a tree carved into his
back) and nobody smirked or accused that show of trying to proselytize. Whether we think in terms of Christ
and the Antichrist, or just good people and bad ones, the struggle between them has made for compelling
drama since the dawn of time.
---
JESUS ALSO COMES UP this week in Showtime's musical version of "Reefer Madness" (Saturday, 8-9:30
p.m.). Or, rather, Jesus comes down off the cross in a satiny white loincloth to sing, in the manner of a Vegas
lounge act: "I'm here to help you . . . and return you to the fold. Try filling your lungs with God, not Jamaican
gold."

--- Page 99 ---
Page 99 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The modern version is an improvement on the original "Reefer Madness," although that's not necessarily
saying much. The 1936 movie about the dangers of marijuana became a cult hit in the 1960s, when mostly
college-age audiences laughed their heads off at its hokey message. But apart from a few over-the-top
moments, in truth the old movie was deadly dull; you had to be stoned to find it that funny.
Showtime's movie musical (based on the stage show that played in Los Angeles in the late 1990s) is a form
of counterpropaganda, its ostensible targets being bigotry and fear. But it more or less echoes the 1936 plot,
with some characters and situations shifted around. A good boy becomes a dope fiend and so, eventually,
does his angelic sweetheart. Then the dope pushers fall out among themselves and blood flows like water.
To add to the camp, in the modern version FDR appears in his wheelchair at the end to save the fallen good
boy from the electric chair.
Since the modern "Reefer Madness" parodies a boring original, it, too, would be yawnsville if its production
numbers didn't liven things up. These range from an amusing dance number in a prison, to a soulful ballad,
as when a dope-den moll addicted to "the stuff" sings about her pusher-boyfriend Jack: "Though the fun
sometimes escapes me, when Jack gets stoned and rapes me . . . . Nothing numbs me better than the stuff."
Then there's the wild, upbeat scene where the formerly angelic heroine, now high on dope, cavorts wantonly
with a bare-bottomed cast in S&M gear. Take that, you uptight morality mongers.
---
THERE'S NOTHING HILARIOUS about drugs in "Rehab," an HBO documentary that follows five young
addicts trying to clean up (Monday, 8:30-10 p.m.). The first boy to tell his story says explicitly that pot was the
initial step on his road to heroin. Yet as the kids share their feelings -- with their parents, and with counselors
at Santa Monica's Camp Recovery -- perhaps the most disquieting discovery for the viewer is that none of
them has had a particularly awful or distorting life. Their woes sound pretty mundane, in fact. What teenager
hasn't been tortured by loneliness and self-doubt at times, or had difficulty communicating with the parental
units?
The scenes in which some of the Camp Recovery residents hook up for instant love affairs, and cut group
meetings until they risk being thrown out of rehab, are particularly provocative. Did drugs make them
screw-ups, or did the drug lifestyle draw them in with its promise of unending drama -- something the sober
life can never deliver? Ultimately all the rehab center manages to do is detox its young residents briefly and
send them otherwise unaltered back into the world. Two years later, the documentary shows us, most are still
sinking fast.
---
"THE DEADLIEST CATCH" is about the men who go out into the Bering Sea each year and for a few brief
days try to make their fortune hauling in Alaskan king crabs. Each week, this wild and wooly Discovery
Channel series (Tuesday, 9-10 p.m.) goes aboard a half dozen or so different ships as their crews work
20-hour shifts, or longer, desperately trying to get "on the catch" -- where the crabs are. Casting hundreds of
700-pound baited cages overboard and reeling them in, on wet decks in high seas, makes king-crab fishing
uniquely hard and dangerous work. Needless to say, it's a man's man's man's man's world.

Gold Prices Fall as Dollar Rises
Commodities Report
Gold Prices Fall as Dollar Rises
By Allen Sykora Dow Jones Newswires
385 words
15 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A surge in the U.S. dollar sent gold and silver futures sharply lower on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange.
Most-active June gold fell $5.40 to $425.60 an ounce, while May silver fell 16.5 cents to $7.06 an ounce.
Spot April gold closed down $5.30 to $424 an ounce.
"It's all dollar-related," said Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management. "You're seeing
selling [by commodity funds]. It started in copper [earlier this week] and is moving over into silver and gold
now."
The dollar has been the main catalyst moving gold for about five years, Mr. Kaplan noted. The metal is often
bought as an alternative to the dollar when the latter is weakening, and vice-versa. The dollar reached its
highest levels against the euro yesterday since February before easing back slightly.
One trader commented that the moves in currencies and metals were brought about by worries that
economies in other parts of the world may slow and be less robust than in the U.S. The trader said when
comparing U.S. economic growth to Europe and Japan, the U.S. dollar is the preferable currency to hold right
now, even with the budget and trade deficits. "Gold and silver have lost their luster as an inflation hedge,"
the trader said.
Traders noted that sell stops -- preplaced orders triggered when certain prices are reached -- were hit in the
June gold futures. The contract bottomed at $423.50 yesterday. When sell stops are uncovered, it can
increase selling momentum and lead to further losses.
"Now, stops are building up in the $423 to $424 range," Mr. Kaplan said.
May silver held at a session low of $7.01. Sell stops were building below the $7 area, said Mr. Kaplan.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Prices rose, supported by gains in heating oil, as the market rebounded from an early drop
below $50 a barrel. May crude-oil futures at the Nymex rose 91 cents to $51.13 a barrel.
COPPER: Futures at the Comex fell for the third straight day, pressured by heavy selling by commodity
funds. May copper fell 3.10 cents to $1.4285 a pound.

Stocks Slump Again Amid Nervousness About Fed Minutes
Monday's Markets
Stocks Slump Again Amid Nervousness About Fed Minutes
By E.S. Browning
394 words
12 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BLUE CHIPS extended their slump into a second day, as investors bit their nails ahead of today's release of
the minutes of the latest Federal Reserve interest-rate meeting.
Investors are nervous because the Fed changed the wording of its post-meeting statement last month,
suggesting growing concerns about inflation. People fear the minutes will indicate building pressure among
Fed policy makers to raise interest rates more sharply. Many people simply are avoiding stocks, and volume
-- 1.22 billion shares on the New York Stock Exchange and 1.33 billion on the Nasdaq Stock Market -- was
the year's lightest at both markets.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 12.78 points, or 0.12%, to 10448.56, down 3.1% so far this year. More
stocks fell than rose on the Big Board. But gains in drug and financial stocks helped the broad Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index inch up 0.01 point -- less than 0.001% -- to 1181.21, off 2.5% this year.
"What I think has really been hurting the market is the Fed. They are talking about raising interest rates
more," to control inflation, said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at New York brokerage firm SG Cowen.
"Now we will see what the Fed was thinking, if they are really seeing inflation."
Investors also await quarterly profit announcements, with General Electric and Citigroup reporting Friday.
Bond investors, who previously had knocked bond prices down because of inflation fears, were surprisingly
calm. Treasury-bond prices rose, pushing yields down.
Gold also rose, and crude oil, down for most of the day, finished with a small gain.
The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.36%, or 7.23 points, to 1992.12, down 8.4% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board 1,420 stocks rose and 1,850 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 12/32, or $3.75 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
down to 4.434%. The 30-year bond was up 21/32 to yield 4.724%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 107.84 yen, down from 108.30 yen Friday, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2976 from $1.2929.

Japanese Foreign-Exchange Reserves Fall
Japanese Foreign-Exchange Reserves Fall
Dow Jones Newswires
303 words
8 April 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOKYO -- Japan's foreign-exchange reserves fell $2.85 billion on a month-to-month basis in March to
$837.72 billion, marking the third straight month of decline, the Ministry of Finance said.
The decrease in Japan's reserves of convertible foreign currencies, gold and International Monetary Fund
special drawing rights followed a contraction of $402 million in February and a decline of $3.58 billion in
January.
Japan's reserves, which ballooned during the finance ministry's aggressive yen-selling, dollar-buying
intervention campaign of January 2003-March 2004, have since posted only minor fluctuations on the back of
interest income, asset price fluctuations and foreign-exchange-rate valuation losses or gains.
In March, the reserves decrease came mainly from the decline in the value of Japan's euro-denominated
assets caused by the single currency's fall against the dollar, a monetary official said.
The euro, which had been trading at $1.3229 at the end of New York trading on the last business day of
February, exited from March trading at $1.2964, according to figures compiled by the ministry.
A rise in U.S. long-term interest rates also pushed down the value of Japan's U.S. Treasury holdings, as
bond prices move in the opposite direction of interest rates, the finance ministry official said.
The yield on 10-year U.S. Treasurys, which stood at 4.381% at the end of February, rose to around 4.486%
during the month, the official said.
Such losses wiped out slight gains from interest income.
Thursday's data also showed Japan's reserves remain the largest in the world.
They were the highest for the 64th straight month in January, which is the latest month for which comparable
data is available.

Your Money Matters (A Special Report): Alternative Investments --- Alternative Answers
Your Money Matters (A Special Report): Alternative Investments --- Alternative Answers
By Gregory Zuckerman
1,119 words
28 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Going Beyond Stocks and Bonds
ALTERNATIVE INVESTING is a hot buzzword (or two) in investing circles these days. But that doesn't mean
investors are always clear what the buzz is all about.
In this regular column, we plan to answer some of your questions about alternative investments. Send them
to reports@wsj.com. In the meantime, here are answers to some of the basics.
Q: What's the allure of alternative investments?
A: The choices investors face today aren't especially attractive. Stocks rallied sharply in 2003 and 2004, after
a three-year slump, and now trade at prices that no longer are cheap. Stocks in the Standard &Poor's
500-stock index, which includes the largest companies, trade a tad above their historic average in relation to
earnings. That suggests stocks are reasonably priced, but not screaming bargains. The market can surely
rise as earnings expand, but investors need to rein in their expectations. Double-digit gains will be harder to
come by in the years ahead since stocks are starting off at full prices.
Bonds aren't much more exciting. Interest rates on all kinds of fixed-income securities -- from super-safe
Treasurys to risky junk bonds -- have come down a lot in recent years, as the Federal Reserve slashed rates.
And even though the Fed is in the process of boosting rates back up, the interest income on many bonds
barely tops inflation. For instance, 10-year Treasury securities yield about 4.6%, compared with an inflation
rate of almost 3%. Bond prices probably will be under pressure as the Fed raises rates.
Given this discouraging environment, many investors are turning to alternative investments -- which refer to
everything from putting money in hedge funds and venture-capital firms to buying real estate, gold, art,
timber and even baseball cards. Some of these investments have turned in great performances in the past
few years, catching the eyes of investors. And it has become easier for smaller investors to move into them.
Unorthodox investment tools that are popular -- like exchange-traded funds, which are mutual funds that
trade like stocks tracking stock or bond indexes -- also qualify as alternative investments.
Q: Are there other reasons to shift into alternative investments?
A: Yes, to diversify a portfolio. So many investors felt deep pain during the brutal bear market of 2000 to 2002
partly because they had too much money in stocks and suffered as prices tumbled. So some investors are
looking for investments that zig when the stock market zags. Bonds help diversify a portfolio, but often bond
prices also move in step with stocks. So far this year, for example, stocks have fallen even as bonds have
lost ground.
Alternative investments can move to their own drummer, however. Gold, for instance, tends to rise in periods
of global tensions, even as stocks tumble, in part because it is seen as a safe haven. Art can go up in value
when the economy roars, but it also can rise in price in more troubled times if inflation is on the rise.
Q: How much of a portfolio should be in alternative investments?
A: That's a toughie. At one time, most experts recommended a basic investment mix of 60% stocks and 40%
bonds, with some adjustments based on an investor's age. But in recent years, more have begun to
appreciate the advantages of having 5% to 15% of a portfolio in alternative investments. In part that's
because some of these investments have done so well -- especially real estate, where home prices as well as
stocks of real-estate investment trusts have climbed.
In general, wealthier investors should put a bigger slice of their portfolios in alternative investments. That's
because some can be volatile. And many of these investments, such as art and timber, can be very illiquid,
meaning it's hard to get in and out of them quickly without incurring a heavy loss. Other such investments, like

--- Page 117 ---
Page 117 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
hedge funds, prohibit withdrawals except for set times during the year. So if an investor might need to pull
money out in a short period of time, such investments aren't ideal.
Also, there tends to be less transparency with alternative investments. A stock price is just a mouse click
away, but it's harder to get an accurate price for that rare stamp or piece of furniture, making some alternative
investments a bit riskier.
Q: So what's the hottest alternative investment right now?
A: Hedge funds. Almost $1 trillion is invested in hedge funds, up from less than $800 million a year ago. The
growth in demand is due, in part, to the fact that many of these funds managed to churn out small gains even
amid the bear market. Hedge funds are private investment partnerships that aim to do well in both rising and
falling markets. In hedge-fund parlance, they strive for "absolute returns." In contrast, mutual funds often are
happy just to beat the market averages.
Hedge funds generally buy investments they think have upside, such as stocks and bonds, while betting
against other investments they think will go down. In that way, they seek to "hedge" themselves and achieve
positive returns in all kinds of markets. Because they can bet against overpriced stocks, or move to the
sidelines when opportunities are scarce, hedge funds tend to be less volatile than mutual funds.
Traditionally, hedge funds were the domain of the super-rich or large institutions such as college
endowments. That's largely because many hedge funds require minimum investments of as much as $1
million, and securities rules demand that investors have an annual salary of $200,000 or net worth of $1
million to be eligible for many hedge funds. But lately many brokerage firms have created innovative
products, such as funds of hedge funds, that have minimum investments as low as $20,000.
But there are big downsides. For one thing, hedge funds charge higher fees than mutual funds and other
investments, usually around 1% or more annually of all assets as well as 20% of any gains. They're also
lightly regulated, and some funds have exaggerated returns or taken on more risk than they promised, though
more scrutiny from the Securities and Exchange Commission is coming.
At the same time, hedge-fund returns have been in line with the market in the past year or so, even as more
money has come into the business, a troubling trend.
---
Mr. Zuckerman is a special writer in The Wall Street Journal's New York bureau. He can be reached at
gregory.zuckerman@wsj.com.

Great Expectations

What a difference a little Federal Reserve determination makes. On Tuesday the Fed finally admitted that it
has been wrong in underestimating rising inflation lo these many months and suggested that more monetary
tightening might well be in its future.
And, what do you know, markets have reacted by bidding up the dollar, dumping gold (which fell by more
than $6 an ounce yesterday to $425.20) and sending oil futures down more than $2 a barrel. The Dow
Jones-AGI Commodity Index fell 2.2% yesterday.
These are all welcome signs of the Fed beginning to cut into what have been rising inflationary expectations.
Intriguingly, those price indicators moved in a healthy direction despite yesterday's Labor Department report
that consumer prices rose 0.4% in February, the highest rate in four months. That suggests that markets
understand that the price indexes are looking through the rear-view mirror, while the Fed's intentions are a
better indicator of actual future prices.
The Fed's apparent new inflation-fighting resolve is of course not universally popular. Various Wall Street
sages and their media echoes are moaning that the Fed is spoiling the party too soon, rather like the barstool
drunk who says how dare the bartender deny him another drink since he's having such a good time. Like the
drunk, the growing U.S. economy will be better off the earlier the sauce of too-easy money is taken away.

will recover only about 25%.

inflation concerns raised by the Fed on Tuesday.

"The story remains fear of inflationary prospects," said Barry Hyman, equity market strategist at Ehrenkrantz
King Nussbaum.

should climb 15% to 20% in fiscal 2006, thanks to "significant product excitement." The company also said
fiscal fourth-quarter earnings fell, thanks to a charge taken to adjust accounting for long-term leases.
