Oil Prices Fall Over $2 as Investors Cut Exposure Amid Firmer Dollar
Commodities Report
Oil Prices Fall Over $2 as Investors Cut Exposure Amid Firmer Dollar
By Masood Farivar Dow Jones Newswires
485 words
24 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Oil prices dropped another 4% as investors continued to flee commodities markets amid a resurgent dollar.
Crude-oil futures for May delivery on the New York Mercantile Exchange dropped as low as $53.40 a barrel
before ending at $53.81 a barrel, down $2.22, on top of a loss of $1.43 Tuesday. Yesterday's drop was the
largest in dollar and percentage terms for a front-month Nymex crude contract in three months.
The sharp decline resulted from a massive liquidation of positions by speculators. Institutional investors such
as mutual funds have turned to commodity futures in recent months on hopes that a weak U.S. currency and
rising global demand will keep commodities on an upward price trajectory.
But as the dollar strengthened against its major counterparts Tuesday following a Federal Reserve
interest-rate increase and continued to surge yesterday on inflation worries, those investors decided to scale
back their exposure to commodity markets in favor of higher-yielding assets such as bonds.
While much of the drop in oil prices was part of the broad weakness in commodities -- the CRB Futures Index
has lost more than 5% since hitting a 24-year high last week -- the oil market received an incentive of its own
for a pullback.
That impetus came in the form of a weekly Energy Information Administration report showing that U.S.
commercial inventories of crude oil grew by a larger-than-expected 4.1 million barrels last week to 309.3
million barrels as imports rose. It was the sixth consecutive weekly build in crude stocks and lifted inventories
well above their five-year average.
Despite the massive exit of speculative money from the energy market, analysts said longer-term
supply-demand concerns will likely lure investors back.
"I think what was going on on Wall Street was more important than the inventory statistics," said Peter Beutel,
president of trading-advisory firm Cameron Hanover in New Canaan, Conn. "We may see more of a
correction, but I still don't think the bull market is over yet."
In other commodity markets:
COCOA: Futures on the New York Board of Trade fell sharply and hit a fresh five-week low as large
speculators liquidated long positions amid a stronger U.S. dollar versus the other major currencies and a
sizeable drop in the Commodity Research Bureau Index. Cocoa for May delivery fell $143 a metric ton, to
$1,588.
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the Nymex fell on large speculator selling and on selling of bullion by
banks. A stronger U.S. dollar against the other major currencies also pressured gold futures. The
most-active April gold contract fell $6.20 a troy ounce, to $425.40, while the illiquid March contract fell $6.10
to $425.20.

Dollar Rallies for Fifth Straight Day On Expectations of Higher Rates
Wednesday's Markets
Dollar Rallies for Fifth Straight Day On Expectations of Higher Rates
By Craig Karmin
1,061 words
24 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOLLAR RALLIED for the fifth straight day, fueled by growing anticipation that the Federal Reserve
could step up the pace in raising interest rates.
Stocks reacted less positively to this prospect. The Dow Jones Industrial Average, off as much as 40 points
during the day, closed down 14.49 points, or 0.1%, to 10456.02 yesterday. Minuscule gains were made by
the Nasdaq Composite Index and Standard &Poor's 500-stock index.
Rate-sensitive financial stocks fell harder than most other stocks. Commodities, including oil and gold prices,
were also notably weaker -- as they often are when the dollar is rising -- and weighed on energy stocks.
Treasury-bond prices, after an initial bout of weakness that sent yields soaring, turned around to close higher
on the day.
Markets were reacting to the Fed's comments on Tuesday, when the central bank announced another
quarter-point rate increase and noted that it was concerned about inflation. A report yesterday showing
consumer prices in February exceeding economists' forecasts added to the perception that rate increases
would accelerate.
The recent gains in the dollar, which hit one-month highs against the euro and the yen, reflect this changing
rate perception. Higher interest rates can attract more money to U.S. securities, especially from hedge funds
and other short-term trades, and that in turn can further boost the dollar.
But looking further ahead, analysts have started to see the potential for some structural concerns related
specifically to the euro.
Chief among them is the possibility France may reject the new European Union constitution. Recent polls
show a slim majority of French voters planning to vote no in a May 29 referendum. While that's far from
certain, a French snub of the constitution would be considered a potentially critical blow to the new document
and would likely undermine confidence in the EU.
It could also trigger a selloff in the currency.
"A French no vote would be a stab in the heart to the euro," says John Taylor, chief investment officer for FX
Concepts, a New York-based hedge fund that specializes in currency trading. "That's the kind of political act
that could serve as a catalyst in causing a currency to fall."
This new concern coincides with another event that many consider a longer-term worry: the recent decision
by European finance ministers to ease limits on budget deficits for the 12 countries participating in the euro.
This sets the stage for greater deficits in major European economies like Germany, France and Italy in efforts
to stimulate their sluggish economies.
Analysts have expressed concern that this could eventually pressure the currency by casting doubt over
Europe's fiscal restraint -- just as the widening U.S. budget deficit has been weighing on the dollar.
Any change in traders' perception of the euro could have wide-ranging effects in the currency market and
beyond. The euro has been the big winner against the dollar, in part because it is the most easily traded
currency. And in sharp contrast to Asian central banks, the ECB has been reluctant to intervene. That has
made the euro a more attractive vehicle for betting against the dollar.
But now that the euro has surged 48% against the U.S. currency in three years -- compared with a 25% gain
by the yen and a 31% rally by the British pound -- some analysts think other currencies stand to gain more.

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We've come to the point where the euro being an obvious alternative to the dollar is nowhere near as clear
as it was two to three years ago," says Robert Sinche, head of global foreign-exchange research and strategy
at Bank of America.
That doesn't mean that the dollar bear market will end soon. Most analysts believe that until the U.S. is able
to control the ballooning trade and budget deficits the dollar will be hard-pressed to enjoy a sustained and
broad-based rally. But it does suggest that other European currencies, like the British pound and Swiss franc,
may continue to rise against the dollar even if the euro falters.
Some analysts see these new concerns as overblown. The French polls have shown a slim majority rejecting
the new constitution, which was designed to streamline EU decision making and help relieve political gridlock.
Many political analysts see this turning around before the vote.
"There's a perception that these things have a tendency to work themselves out," says Steven Englander, a
foreign-exchange analyst for Barclays Capital in New York. "The market hasn't factored in a no vote."
Others see that as part of the problem. Mr. Taylor at FX Concepts thinks if by May French polls still show a
negative as likely, or even possible, it will begin to take a toll on the euro.
Further down the road, Europe's decision to ease limits on budget deficits could become a recurring problem
for the currency. In a press release on the ECB Web site this week, the central bank warned that changes to
the budget limits "undermine confidence in the fiscal framework of the European Union."
The new rules create additional loopholes, such as a protracted period of very low growth, that allow
members' budget deficits to exceed the limit of 3% of a country's gross domestic product. Economists fear
that more bloated deficits could spark inflation and eventually lead to higher interest rates.
"This proposed reform introduces a higher degree of economic judgment and inevitably becomes a potential
risk," wrote Lorenzo Codogno, co-head of European economics for Bank of America.
Mr. Taylor notes that the dollar comes under renewed selling pressure whenever government figures are
released showing an ever wider trade deficit. He wonders if expanding European budget deficits won't start to
have a similar effect on the euro.
In major U.S. market action yesterday:
Stocks were mixed and bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note was up 7/32, or $2.19 for each $1,000
invested, to push down the yield to 4.6%. The 30-year bond rose 21/32 to yield 4.858%.
The dollar strengthened. The currency rose to 106.09 yen, up from 105.56, while the euro fell to $1.2976 from
$1.3088.

Page 126 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

Greenspan's Seventh Shot --- The Fed Likely Will Raise Rates, Building on Six Other Increases; Stocks Fall and the Dollar Rallies
Monday's Markets
Greenspan's Seventh Shot --- The Fed Likely Will Raise Rates, Building on Six Other Increases;
Stocks Fall and the Dollar Rallies
By Craig Karmin
734 words
22 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS STOPPED worrying about higher oil prices just long enough to start fretting again about higher
interest rates.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average stumbled 64.28 points, or 0.6%, to its lowest finish since Feb. 1, while the
Nasdaq Composite Index retreated to its lowest close since Nov. 3. The losses came ahead of the Federal
Reserve's pivotal meeting today; the Fed's policy makers are expected to raise interest rates, and
economists are divided on whether the Fed will signal that the pace of rate increases is about to pick up.
The increased chance that the Fed will accelerate rate increases helped lift the dollar yesterday to its highest
level in two weeks against the euro, with its biggest one-day gain since Jan. 4, as investors speculated that
higher rates would make U.S. bonds yields more attractive than foreign-bond yields.
Comments from the Hong Kong Monetary Authority's Joseph Yam also contributed to the dollar's third
straight day of gains. Mr. Yam said Asian central banks shouldn't endanger financial markets by selling off
dollars to buy more euros too quickly.
Recent signs that Asian central banks are looking to boost their euro holdings at the expense of dollars have
been weighing on the U.S. currency for the past several weeks. Oil prices slipped for only the third time over
the past 14 sessions. Gold prices, which tend to move in the opposite direction of the dollar, slid 1.9% for
their second-worst day this year.
The Dow industrials closed at 10565.39, leaving the blue-chip index down 2% for the year. The broad
Standard &Poor's 500-stock index slid 0.5%, or 5.87 points, to 1183.78, off 2.3% this year. The Nasdaq
Composite Index fought its way back into positive territory in the afternoon but closed down 0.01%, or 0.28
point, at 2007.51. The tech-stock-heavy index is 7.7% lower for the year.
The Fed is widely expected to raise the benchmark federal-funds rate by a quarter-point to 2.75%, the
seventh interest-rate increase since the central bank began the current tightening cycle in June. An
announcement typically comes at 2:15 p.m. EST.
Yet since the Fed's policy-setting committee previous meeting in February, concerns about inflation have
intensified: Oil prices have moved up steadily, hitting a 22-year high, while the broad commodity index set a
24-year high. A weaker dollar has raised the cost of imports; and job growth has been strong, raising the
prospect that wages may accelerate.
All this has raised the likelihood that the Fed will announce its intention to accelerate the pace of future rate
increases to ward off inflation. J.P. Morgan put the odds at nearly 50% that the Fed indicates this will be the
case.
"There's a lot more attention being paid to what the Fed is going to say this time," notes David Sowerby, a
portfolio manager for Loomis Sayles.
Stocks came under pressure from the start, as oil prices began the day up again, with crude rising above $57
a barrel despite assurances from Saudi Arabia that oil producers could decide at any time to add more
supply.
When oil prices peaked around midday and then began to fall, stocks bounced off their bottom and tried to
rally. But traders said there is a reluctance to buy stocks with this much uncertainty before the Fed meeting.
"Today had a lot of developments that would normally be bullish for stocks," said Steve Massocca, co-chief
executive at Pacific Growth Equities in San Francisco. He referred to the dollar's rally and a spurt of merger

--- Page 130 ---

activity that included IAC/InterActiveCorp.'s agreement to buy search site Ask Jeeves Inc. in a stock deal
valued at $1.85 billion. "But none of this seemed to impress the market with the Fed weighing so heavily," he
said.

$1.3313.

Dollar Moves Up; Hong Kong Aide Helps Fuel Climb
Currency Trading
Dollar Moves Up; Hong Kong Aide Helps Fuel Climb
By Laurence Norman Dow Jones Newswires
525 words
22 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar made sharp gains, rising to a three-week high versus the euro ahead of today's Federal Reserve
policy-making meeting.
Along with Fed anticipation, the dollar got a boost from reports of comments from Hong Kong Monetary
Authority chief Joseph Yam, who acknowledged the growing popularity of the euro, but said any moves by
central banks to diversify into that currency too quickly would threaten financial markets. The U.S. currency
has been vulnerable of late to any talk about central-bank dollar diversification.
The Fed is widely expected to lift its target rate by one-quarter point to 2.75%. Speculation officials may
remove a reference to tightening monetary policy at a "measured pace" helped boost the dollar.
Yet what goes up can also come down. If the Fed sticks to the status quo, the U.S. currency could struggle,
at least initially, to hold on to its gains, analysts say.
Late yesterday in New York, the euro was at $1.3170 from $1.3313 late Friday. The dollar was at 105.09 yen
from 104.71 yen and at 1.1777 Swiss francs from 1.1647 francs. The pound was at $1.8987 from $1.9204,
while the euro was at 138.37 yen from 139.41 yen.
The dollar's gains appeared to ripple into other markets. Hedge-fund selling helped to push the April gold
contract on Comex down $8.20 to $430.10.
Markets were closed in Japan and there were no major U.S. data. The dollar's move followed a range of
factors overnight. The euro took a hit from news that European Union finance ministers agreed over the
weekend to relax the terms of the stability and growth pact.
The dollar rally surprised traders who were holding short-dollar positions -- betting it would fall -- and that
helped extend the climb by forcing investors to square those positions, said John McCarthy, director of
foreign exchange at ING Barings Capital Markets in New York.
The dollar got an extra bounce when it pushed sterling below key support at $1.9180 overnight and when it
forced the euro below $1.3180 in early New York trading.
Yet speculation about the Fed was also an important aspect of the dollar's climb. Traders bought the U.S.
currency on the back of growing speculation the central bank may drop the phrase "measured pace" from its
statement. The Fed has been talking about tightening monetary policy at a measured pace since May 2004,
the meeting before it lifted rates by a quarter point from a historic low of 1%.
So far, that is exactly how it has happened. But as interest rates rise, they have gotten closer to the central
bank's paramount goal of having policy in a position where it neither stimulates nor restricts growth. Officials
have been evasive in defining that exact level, but some have allowed that a federal-funds rate (which
governs overnight interbank loans) of 3% to 5% could fit that bill.
---
Michael Derby contributed to this article.

Gold Falls 1.8% as Dollar Climbs

Gold futures fell more than $8 an ounce, or about 1.8%, on heavy selling spurred by a stronger dollar.
The most active April gold contract at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange settled $8.30

The decline was the second-biggest of the year and biggest loss since Jan 3.
A majority of April's slide occurred at the open, after spot gold prices tumbled to nearly two-week lows during
the Asian and European sessions.
The dollar's steep climb against the euro and other rivals was the primary driver behind the selloff. The
increase rendered dollar-alternatives such as gold less attractive and spurred fund players with short
positions in the U.S. dollar and long exposure to gold to reverse portions of those trades.
"The dollar's move higher started this all off, so what the dollar does next will be key for gold," argued Scott
Meyers, technical analyst and dealer at Pioneer Futures in New York.
Investors are expected to adjust their positions further across asset classes today ahead of the Federal
Reserve's decision on interest rates.
Larry Young, senior dealer at Infinity Brokerage Services in Chicago, said that while the heavy price fall
suggests the market's bias is to the downside for the near term, any further weakness should be viewed as a
buying opportunity. "There're still reasons to like gold, and I expect a rebound soon," he said.
In other commodity trading:

dollar and heavy selling by funds. The May soybean contract lost 22.5 cents to settle at $6.26 1/2 a bushel.

CRUDE OIL: Futures at the Nymex eased, closing below $57 on signs that OPEC may raise its production
ceiling by 500,000 barrels a day. Benchmark light, sweet crude futures for April delivery fell 10 cents from its
Nymex-record close to finish at $56.62 in its last day of trading, just its third decline in the past 14 sessions.

Greenspan's Real Mistake
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Greenspan's Real Mistake
1,063 words
21 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Alan Greenspan has taken a political beating for his recent comments on fiscal policy, which isn't even his
job. The more important question, as the Federal Reserve's Open Market Committee meets again this week,
is whether the Chairman and his mates have made a monetary policy blunder that is reigniting inflation.
The markets are certainly beginning to wonder. After months of heeding Mr. Greenspan's public assurances
that there's no problem, investors got the inflation jitters last week. The Nasdaq hit a low for the year, oil
reached a new high at $57 a barrel, while gold and other commodity prices continue to soar. As for
real-estate speculation, a friend of ours quips that in some U.S. markets "they're day-trading lots."
Friday's report on import prices showed a 0.8% rise for February following 0.7% in January. While most of
that is due to oil, non-petroleum import prices are also up 2.9% in the past year. A reasonable conclusion is
that the Fed's easy money policy is reducing the value of the dollar and prompting importers to raise prices.
The Fed is going to have to tighten money to get control of this incipient inflation, and the faster it does the
less financial damage there is likely to be down the road.
---
We should stress that the Greenspan Fed's overall performance is one reason the U.S. economy has done
as well as it has in recent years. Leave aside the debate about whether the Fed should have, or could have,
done something to deflate the 1990s' stock-market bubble. Once that bubble burst, and then confronted with
September 11, the Fed printed more dollars to keep consumers flush and compensate for tapped-out
businesses. This is one reason the 2001 recession was among the shallowest on record.
The Fed's mistake has been to keep the dollar presses running at full throttle for too long. As recently as late
2003 and amid surging GDP growth, some Fed governors were still warning about the risks of "deflation."
Like most other conventional economists, the Fed gurus greatly underestimated the pro-growth impact of the
Bush 2003 tax cuts. Though he began raising short-term interest rates in June 2004, Mr. Greenspan has
tolerated negative real rates for three years. This is the most accommodative monetary policy since the
1970s, and it has begun to produce a revival in inflation expectations.
For the past year, markets have been reassured by both the Fed's own rhetoric that inflation was under
control and by the price indexes. But the consumer price index as well as Mr. Greenspan's favorite Personal
Consumption Expenditure (PCE) deflator are lagging indicators. The other measure that has confounded
investors, and maybe the Fed itself, is the long-bond rate. The 10-year Treasury note has remained
remarkably low despite rising short-term rates, a fact that some have interpreted as a sign of no inflation. Mr.
Greenspan recently declared the 10-year note trend to be a "conundrum."
A better explanation is the "carry trade," or the habit of hedge funds and others to ride the yield curve by
borrowing short at lower rates and then lending long at higher rates to make a buck. As short rates start to
rise, the carry-traders get squeezed and their initial response tends to be to grow the size of the order book
as long as a profit can still be made. But sooner or later the carry-trade margin vanishes, the investments
have to be unwound, and the bid side for the long bond evaporates. That's when the bond bears cash in their
bets as long rates climb.
Our own habit of many years has been to look at gold and other commodity prices, including the dollar, as
better forward-looking inflation indicators. They've been flashing red for some time, and while they fell for a
time last year as the Fed finally seemed to be acting, these prices have rebounded again as our central
bankers have fallen behind the curve.
We aren't the only ones warning about inflation risks. No less an authority than Paul Volcker, the former Fed
Chairman, used a February speech at Stanford to underscore what he called "the oldest lesson of economic
policy: a strong sense of monetary and fiscal discipline. . . . This is not a time to take chances on forcing

--- Page 137 ---
Page 137 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
faster economic growth at the risk of rising inflation." Mr. Volcker has rarely spoken up about monetary
policy since he left the Fed in 1987, so this is stout ale.
---
Slowly, the Fed seems to be recognizing its mistake, as have financial markets. The latter are now
anticipating an increase of 50 basis points in the short-term rates influenced by the Fed sometime soon,
rather than the usual 25. But our advice would be for the Fed to do whatever it takes to demonstrate its
anti-inflation resolve, draining dollar liquidity as needed through open-market operations, and perhaps
adding a sharper and more immediate interest-rate jolt. No doubt some financial players will be caught by
surprise, and both the Treasury and FDIC will have to be on alert, but some damage now is inevitable given
the Fed's anti-inflation tardiness.
At least the overall economy continues to be healthy, as the incentives from tax cuts have ignited a buoyant
recovery in business spending. First quarter U.S. GDP growth should hit 4%, job creation is strong, and
federal revenues are rising at a nearly 10% year-over-year rate. One lesson of the last 20 years is that price
stability has helped to smooth out the business cycle, as expansions become longer when they aren't
undermined by rising inflation.
The main threat to this current expansion isn't the trade deficit, the budget deficit, the savings rate or any of
the other media fads. The real danger is a renewal of inflation that forces interest rates higher than they
would otherwise have to be. As Mr. Greenspan prepares for the end of his distinguished Fed tenure next
year, surely he can't want such a policy mistake to be his final legacy.
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: No Cause to Berate Fed For `Reigniting Inflation'" -- WSJ April 7,
2005)

In Praise of Michael Eisner
In Praise of Michael Eisner
By Edward Jay Epstein
704 words
18 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The press will no longer have Michael Eisner to kick around. After becoming an irresistible magnet for
disparaging stories in the past decade, he announced last week that he is retiring as head of Disney in
September. At a minimum, he had a real separation problem with the associates he axed. His bad press
began in 1994 soon after he forced out the talented studio chief, Jeffrey Katzenberg, who then sued Disney
for additional compensation; greatly intensified in 1997 after he fired -- and paid off with $140 million --
Michael Ovitz; and turned into a media tsunami in 2003 after he kicked Roy Disney (old Walt's nephew) and
Stanley Gold off the board.
The onslaught of criticism ranged from citing his name-calling -- Mr. Katzenberg was a "midget" and Mr. Ovitz
a "psychopath" -- to his second-guessing decisions about movies, e.g. his passing on distributing the
crowd-pleasing, pseudo-documentary "Fahrenheit 9/11." Stories about his bad behavior were sufficiently
plentiful to allow journalists equipped with moral compasses to locate his "dark side."
What is lost in the stories about Mr. Eisner's arrogance, greed and insensitivity is the more illuminating tale of
how he transformed a faltering animation and amusement park company into one of the world's most
successful home entertainment companies. When he assumed command in 1984, Disney had a market value
of $1.8 billion. Today, its market value is $57.1 billion. A 30-fold increase in value is no minor accomplishment
in the mercurial entertainment economy -- indeed, one in which News Corporation and Vivendi-Universal
almost went bankrupt, and Time-Warner, Viacom and Sony were forced to take multibillion-dollar write-offs.
Even correcting for inflation, Disney increased 19 times in value between 1984 and 2005.
The answer to how Mr. Eisner, despite his many tactical errors, added $55 billion in value to Disney lay in his
strategic vision that Disney's future would be in home entertainment not movie theaters. Consider just two
decisions he made that brought about this incredible corporate transformation. First, in the mid-'80s, he
released Disney's library of animated movies on video over the strenuous objections of many, including Mr.
Katzenberg, who opposed the move on the grounds that it would kill their value as re-releases in movie
houses. Within a few years, with its animated features accounting for seven of the 10 top-selling videos,
Disney had found a new El Dorado. By the early-'90s, video sales were providing almost all of the movie
division profits. Skipping theaters entirely, Disney now releases sequels to features such as "The Lion King"
directly on video.
Second, in 1995, Mr. Eisner totally reshaped Disney by acquiring Capital Cities/ABC for $19 billion. With this
coup, Disney not only got 10 TV stations in the biggest advertising markets and one of the three broadcast
networks, it got control of ESPN, the single most profitable cable network. Since cable operators needed this
sports network to attract subscribers, ESPN was able to ratchet up its charge for cable operators intercepting
its satellite signals to $1.75 per subscriber per month. This effective tax on cable households -- and ESPN's
ability to raise it by 20% a year -- gives Disney (which owns 80% of ESPN) not only an enormous cash flow
($1.1 billion in 2004) but, together with The Disney Channel, leverage over the entire cable industry. Mr.
Eisner had moved Disney to the forefront of home entertainment.
In many ways, his corporate success also proved his undoing. The institutions that own two-thirds of Disney's
shares came to focus obsessively on his inability to deliver a stock-market payoff. The writing was on the wall
last March after 43% of shareholders voted to withhold their support from him. So Michael Eisner picked
Robert Iger, current president of Disney, as his successor. Free of his boss's accumulated baggage -- and
especially of his bad press -- Mr. Iger will presumably continue Disney's transformation. And we can all be
sure of one thing: He will be using the Eisner template.
---
Mr. Epstein is the author of "The Big Picture" just out from Random House.

Oil Companies Reap Cash Windfall From Price Surge --- Investors Say Big Stockpiles Can Help Pump Up Returns; Exxon's $23.1 Billion Hoard
Oil Companies Reap Cash Windfall From Price Surge --- Investors Say Big Stockpiles Can Help Pump
Up Returns; Exxon's $23.1 Billion Hoard
By Jeffrey Ball and Russell Gold
1,291 words
18 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The surge in oil prices over the past year is generating an immense cascade of cash that is flooding the
coffers of oil companies, raising pressure on their bosses to pursue acquisitions, further boost spending on
production or give more of the gains to shareholders.
So far, big Western oil companies have been reluctant to commit the bulk of that cash toward any of those
ends, over concern that the rise in oil prices will prove temporary. They argue that big acquisitions could lose
value if oil prices drop, and say it's not worth pouring even their extra resources into further development if
returns don't justify the expense -- despite rising competition from oil-hungry nations like China and India.
Moreover, though the cash windfall is large -- Exxon Mobil Corp., the world's biggest oil company, ended
2004 with $23.1 billion in cash on hand, more than double its hoard from a year earlier -- plans at most oil
companies assume prices will drop from today's highs.
But some shareholders say they should get a big share of that windfall -- perhaps with a special dividend like
the one Microsoft Corp. paid investors last year, worth $32.6 billion. With the tax rate on dividends at 15%,
and the possibility that could rise in the future, Frederick Leuffer, an oil analyst at Bear Stearns, says a
special payment this year is preferable to incremental boosts over several years.
Investors also say they're concerned because cash sitting on a company's books typically earns far less than
when it's invested in an oil or natural-gas project. Despite company assertions that they have plenty of oil left
to pump, some investors argue the industry lacks investment options in parts of the world that have the most
fossil fuel -- particularly the Middle East.
To be sure, most oil companies have been boosting regular dividends and buying back shares; Exxon, for
instance, raised spending on dividends and buybacks to $14.9 billion in 2004 from $11.5 billion a year earlier.
Additionally, at least two large companies, ChevronTexaco Corp. and Eni SpA, are said to be considering a
deal for Unocal Corp., which could be valued at $17 billion.
In response to questions from investors, industry executives preach patience. At a meeting with analysts last
week in New York, Exxon Chairman and Chief Executive Lee Raymond said the company is carefully
watching spending and doesn't plan a major acquisition soon. Exxon officials say a one-time special dividend
wouldn't do much to boost the long-term value of the stock.
"Oil is a commodity," Mr. Raymond said. "It's hard to identify a single commodity that has maintained a very
high price over a very long period of time."
Exxon's not alone in seeing its hoard of cash -- that is, the amount of money left over after paying all
expenses, from capital projects to taxes -- grow as oil prices climb higher. At Royal Dutch/Shell Group, cash
on hand rose to $8.5 billion in 2004, from $2 billion a year earlier. ChevronTexaco's cash jumped to $9.3
billion from $4.3 billion.
Together, the world's five biggest oil companies -- which include BP PLC and Total SA besides Exxon, Royal
and ChevronTexaco -- generated $67.4 billion in free cash flow in 2004, according to Norwalk, Conn.,
energy-research firm John S. Herold. That figure refers to the amount of money left after capital spending, but
before paying dividends and buying back shares. The 2004 figure, boosted by some asset sales, was up
nearly 32% from the $51.1 billion generated in 2003.
Herold estimates the five companies will throw off just $55.5 billion in free cash flow in 2005 -- but that
estimate is premised on an average oil price of $40 a barrel for the year. Right now, oil is priced much higher,
driven steadily upward by surging demand, in the U.S. and in growing economies like China's, even as the
industry pumps about as hard as it can.

--- Page 141 ---
Page 141 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yesterday, crude oil for April delivery finished the day at $56.40, down six cents, after hitting a new intraday
record in trading on the New York Mercantile Exchange of $57.60. That figure remains behind historical highs
when adjusted for inflation.
Herold predicts Exxon will be the industry's biggest generator of free cash this year, racking up another $16.4
billion. Even allowing for a moderate increase this year in capital spending, share buybacks and the regular
dividend, Exxon's cash pile is likely to rise to at least $35 billion by the end of the year, estimates Fadel Gheit,
an oil analyst at Oppenheimer &Co. That would amount to a roughly 50% increase in Exxon's cash in 2005
alone.
Mr. Gheit calculates that every $1 increase in the average price per barrel of oil adds about $550 million to
Exxon's after-tax annual profit. If its expenses don't change, that additional profit all piles up as cash -- a
problem, Mr. Gheit says, that shows up in a company's "return on capital employed." That common industry
measure is meant to capture how effectively the company's capital produces profit.
Though high oil prices boost the short-term profits of oil companies, they make it tougher for companies to
produce enough new oil and natural gas to replenish what is pumped out of the ground. Under agreements
that are becoming increasingly common in the oil industry, governments that own much of the remaining big
fossil-fuel stashes give Western oil companies proportionately less fossil fuel as the price of those
commodities rises.
Industry executives have reason to watch spending. When oil prices surged in the 1970s, companies sought
new investments, as when the former Mobil Corp. bought Montgomery Ward and Occidental Petroleum Corp.
acquired Iowa Beef Processors. Neither purchase succeeded, and each company ended up shedding its
acquisition.
Even so, if executives are wrong, and prices keep rising and stay high, the companies may well be forced to
rethink their current approach. If they don't, they could lose potential assets to smaller competitors, including
state-controlled oil companies in the developing world, that are hungrier for growth.
The cash windfall goes well beyond the industry giants. Burlington Resources Inc., a Houston-based
independent, reported last month that it had $2.18 billion in cash and cash equivalents at the end of 2004,
nearly triple the amount it had a year earlier. Steven Shapiro, Burlington's chief financial officer, told analysts
in January that his company was "interested" in acquisitions but wouldn't aggressively ramp up capital
spending because that tends to hurt returns. He acknowledged that the company was nearing a point at
which, if it didn't find an acquisition in 2005, adding more cash would become "inefficient," and promised to
address the issue.
Exxon's Mr. Raymond, though downplaying the likelihood his company would make a major purchase soon,
hasn't ruled one out down the road. Indeed, in signaling that Exxon probably will raise its rate of share
buybacks, he recalled at last week's meeting that Exxon has used buybacks in previous periods of high oil
prices to amass equity that the company later used to pounce when prices fell.
One such buyback started in the early 1980s, he recalled. Exxon ended up repurchasing one billion shares at
a cost of $16 billion. In 1999, it used them to buy Mobil for about $80 billion.
"A pretty good investment for the shareholders," Mr. Raymond said.

Stocks Ease After Oil Hits High, Falls Back
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Ease After Oil Hits High, Falls Back
By Ian McDonald
628 words
18 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SEEMS LIKE EVERY time stocks try to get some traction, they hit an oil patch.
The price of crude hit a 22-year-high, $57.60 per barrel, at one point yesterday, before slipping back late in
the afternoon. The commodity finished down six cents at $56.40 on the New York Mercantile Exchange, but
is up 48% over the past 12 months. Steep oil prices hurt stocks because higher energy costs take a bite out
of corporate profits and can spur inflation.
Oil's high price boosted shares of energy companies but sapped interest in most stocks.The indexes spent
much of the day drifting in and out of the black, finishing almost exactly where they started.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished down 6.72 points, or 0.06%, to 10626.35, leaving it a little over
1% in the red so far this year. The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index inched up 2.14 points to 1190.21,
while the tech-laden Nasdaq Composite was up less than one point to 2016.42. Those indexes are also
underwater since Jan. 1.
"It was a real tug of war today to stay in positive territory," said Andy Brooks, head of trading at T. Rowe Price
Group Inc in Baltimore. "It seems like oil is the game of choice among speculators and there are other
tremors out there, like General Motors."
The auto maker said Wednesday that it would miss its earnings targets widely, making investors jittery about
prospects for the company's shares, its bonds and the economy overall. Investors' worries overshadowed
better-than-expected readings on jobless claims and the economy, in addition to more reports of outsize
profits from Wall Street investment banks.
Exxon Mobil Corp. gained 2% to close at $61.49 on the Big Board, better than any other Dow component.
The worst performer in the Dow was insurer American International Group Inc., which is tangling with
regulators; its shares fell 3.3% to $60.80 on the Big Board yesterday and are now down 6% since investors
learned on Monday that its longtime chief executive, Maurice "Hank" Greenberg, was giving up that post and
becoming nonexecutive chairman.
Traders expect far more lively markets today, a so-called quadruple-witching day, when four types of options
and futures contracts expire. These expirations usually trigger a spate of buying and selling as institutional
investors shift their portfolios.
Also stirring the pot tomorrow will be the first half of a rejiggering of the S&P 500 to make it reflect only shares
that are actively trading, not those held by insiders that can't easily be traded. That's scheduled to happen
after the close of trading, so S&P 500 index-fund managers will need to retool their portfolios, prompting a
spate of anticipatory trading.
"It's a major change to the index and it will roil things," says Steve Sachs, director of trading at Rydex
Investments in Rockville, Md. "It will be a busy day."
Stocks' woes raised interest in long-term Treasury bonds, whose prices rose. The dollar rose against the euro
and the yen, while the price of gold fell.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.58 billion shares traded, 1,876 stocks rose and 1,375 fell.
Bonds rose. The 10-year Treasury note rose 10/32, or $3.125 per $1,000 invested, pushing the yield down to
4.468%. The 30-year bond rose 16/32 to yield 4.759%.

Business and Finance
Business and Finance

18 March 2005

A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FEDEX REPORTED a 53% jump in quarterly profit on a 21% rise in revenue, helped by strong growth in both
U.S. ground shipments and international air deliveries. But high fuel costs could have an impact on results in

Common Sense: Commodity Prices Are Signaling It's Time for an Inflation Hedge
Personal Finance
Common Sense: Commodity Prices Are Signaling It's Time for an Inflation Hedge
By James B. Stewart SmartMoney
689 words
16 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
YOU HAVE PROBABLY NOTICED that oil prices are hovering near a record $55 a barrel level again. But
have you checked the price of copper, aluminum, cotton, orange juice or cocoa lately?
All five are part of the Commodities Research Board's price indexes. Oil, while expensive, is only getting back
to the price it hit last fall. But the Reuters-CRB Futures Index is at a 25-year high.
Something strange is going on in the markets. Even as Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan assures us that
inflation is under control and long-term interest rates remain near historical lows, commodity prices are
going through the roof. So far the increases aren't showing up much in consumer prices, thanks to
competition and greater manufacturing efficiency. But something has to give eventually -- either raw-materials
prices have to drop or interest rates have to rise, or both.
This is one of the main reasons I have lowered my exposure to longer-term fixed-income investments, like
Treasurys and corporate and municipal bonds. A rise in long-term rates seems about as inevitable as it gets
in the unpredictable world of investing.
But what about the inflation side of the equation? I've been saying for some time that investors need an
inflation hedge. Treasury inflation-protected securities are popular -- but TIPS are still bonds, and their
yields remain low.
Commodities trading isn't practical for most individual investors. But there are alternatives. I have had a
natural-resources mutual fund for many years. During the near-deflationary years that ended just over a year
ago, it went nowhere. But it has done well this past year, even though it mostly holds shares in manufacturing
companies, which are tied more to the economic cycle than to commodities prices. There are also several
basic-materials and natural-resources exchange-traded funds that serve the same purpose.
Lately I have become intrigued by two companies that let investors come closer to owning commodities than
do any mutual funds or ETFs, most of which include shares in the major oil producers. While it can serve as
an inflation hedge, oil tends to be even more dependent on the economic cycle, not to mention the often
unpredictable moves of OPEC. But BHP Billiton and Rio Tinto, both with dual headquarters in London and
Melbourne, Australia, are much more tied to commodity prices.
BHP's assets are impressive: aluminum, coal, copper, iron ore, titanium, nickel, diamonds, silver, oil, gas and
liquefied natural gas. BHP, one of the world's largest natural-resources companies, is currently bidding for
Australian rival WMC Resources, and so is Rio Tinto. Rio Tinto also boasts an enviable array of commodities:
aluminum, copper, diamonds, coal, uranium, gold, titanium and iron ore.
Given the recent run-up in commodity prices, neither of these stocks is cheap. Both trade on the New York
Stock Exchange as American depositary receipts. BHP was trading recently at near $30 a share, just off its
52-week high of $31. Rio Tinto was near $138, close to its high of nearly $144. BHP has more than doubled
in the past year, and Rio Tinto is close. Still, forward price/earnings ratios are a reasonable 17 and 12,
respectively.
I would wait for at least a modest pullback before buying, but long term, both companies offer a good
inflation hedge. Because they are foreign companies with many of their businesses outside the U.S., they
should also gain from a falling dollar.
They are also beneficiaries of a strong global economy, and especially from strong commodities demand
from China. So they offer an entry into three of today's hottest investment themes: rising inflation, a falling
dollar and strength in emerging markets. Not many companies can rival that.

Albania Faces `Underground' Challenge
Albania Faces `Underground' Challenge
By Nick Carey Dow Jones Newswires
1,426 words
16 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2E
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tirana, Albania -- Kel, who run a kebab shop near Skanderberg Square in the center of Tirana, doesn't pay
taxes, doesn't use banks and doesn't keep books.
Like thousands of other Albanians, he likes the freedom of operating in the country's underground economy.
But he is worried it will take years to expand his business. "I can't get a bank loan to open a second kebab
shop," says the 23-year-old entrepreneur who declines to give his full name.
Albania's challenge is to persuade successful businessmen such as Kel that a fledgling
government-regulated financial system can be trusted and will benefit their businesses. If the government
succeeds, a trickle of hardy bankers and investors that have ventured into the former communist state could
gain momentum, boosting foreign investment.
So far, the government has made modest progress, most notably by persuading its own ministries to start
using banks to pay salaries. Finance Minister Arben Malaj says the government is determined to build from
scratch a "proper working state" -- a tax system people trust, along with government services such as health
care and pensions.
It will be an uphill struggle, financial experts say, because of the extent of the country's underground
economy, lingering concern about government corruption and skepticism toward financial investments after a
massive fraud left many investors penniless in the 1990s.
The International Monetary Fund estimates that returns from unregistered trading -- ranging from street
vendors to illegal money changers -- could amount to as much as 30% of Albania's gross domestic product,
or the total value of all goods and services produced in a nation.
"This is a country of small entrepreneurs of a scale that I have never seen before," says Nadir Mohammed,
Albanian country manager of the World Bank, whose previous postings have included the Middle East and
Africa. "But unfortunately much of that entrepreneurship takes place outside the system."
During the past decade, both international lenders have allocated a total of more than $800 million to Albania
in an effort to bolster a fragile free-market economy.
The crush of sellers calling out from Tirana's potholed streets displays the immensity of the informal market.
Few have government business licenses and most decline to give their full names for fear of being denied
government benefits or being forced to pay taxes.
Ishmail, 27 years old, rents his mobile phone starting at the equivalent of 30 european cents, or about U.S. 40
cents, a minute for local calls to those who can't afford a monthly rental fee -- or choose not to be registered
as owning a phone and thus identified as having an income that could be taxed.
Hakan, 72, charges passersby the equivalent of 50 european cents to weigh themselves on his bathroom
scales -- a luxury for many in Albania. He says he needs the money to supplement a state pension that
doesn't cover his rent.
Ina, 48, hawks plastic grocery bags full of bras for about 5 euros, or $6.75, a bag. Flashing gold teeth, she
says she has four children to support. Her husband has been working illegally in Germany for five years, she
adds, sending her money that she hides at home so they can buy a house when he returns. "Another year,
maybe two, then he'll come back," she says before spotting a potential buyer and moving off, waving her
bags and yelling.
For 45 years Albanians labored under one of the most reclusive and repressive communist regimes in central
and eastern Europe. When the regime collapsed in 1990, a loosely regulated financial sector and uneducated
consumers were exploited by investment companies pitching pyramid schemes -- which attract investors by

--- Page 150 ---
Page 150 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
offering high returns that are paid to the first investors using funds received from later investors. Many
Albanians, who invested a total of $1.2 billion in the funds, rioted when the funds folded, causing the
government to collapse. Although there were many arrests, only a few fund managers received prison
sentences and few investors received any compensation.
As a result, although many Albanians embrace a free-market economy, they have yet to invest their
livelihoods in the government-regulated economy. Because the profits from this frenetic capitalism remain
largely untaxed, the government must show entrepreneurs that if they do pay taxes, the revenue will be used
for their benefit.
The director of the central bank, Ardian Fullani, promises to turn more Albanians into taxpaying citizens by
instituting transparent financial regulations and communicating the benefits of joining the system. "If we show
companies the benefits of joining the formalized economy then lending will grow dramatically," Mr. Fullani
says. "In order to do that, we have to explain that paying taxes can pave the way to funding sources."
One early step has been to phase out the government's own policy of bypassing banks. Until recently,
officials all the way up to the prime minister were paid in cash. Gradually, wage payments to the state's
110,000 bureaucrats are to be handled through the country's banks. This process is expected to take until
mid-2006.
Government-worker deposits eventually should enable banks to lend money to entrepreneurs now operating
in the underground economy, says Steven Grunerud, chief executive of Raiffeisen Bank in Albania, a unit of
Raiffeisen Zentralbank Oesterreich AG of Vienna. "With proof of income, we can lend them small amounts at
first and build up to long-term loans such as mortgages," says Mr. Grunerud, whose bank bought Albania's
largest savings bank, Banka e Kursimeve, from the government in December 2003 for $126 million.
The biggest challenge, foreign financial experts say, is to overhaul the government's tax system -- primarily
customs duties charged on imported goods. Jan-Peter Olters, the IMF's representative in Tirana, says the tax
system is so poorly run that the government is frequently forced to cut programs from the budget midyear
when it becomes clear projected tax revenue won't be forthcoming. "This makes fiscal planning difficult at
best," he says, adding that the problem is the biggest single barrier to further foreign investment in Albania.
Allegations of bribery and other forms of corruption also make entrepreneurs and investors leery.
In 2004, Albania ranked 108th out of 146 countries on the Corruption Perception Index of international
nongovernment organization Transparency International, which asks international institutions and others to
rank the degree of corruption in individual markets. The ranking put Albania just ahead of Argentina, Libya
and the Palestinian Authority.
Albania faces "an alarming situation, where corruption infects institutions, political parties and the entire
society," Kreshnik Spahiu, head of Albanian NGO Citizen's Advocacy Office, said in November when signing
a partnership agreement with Transparency International aimed at promoting transparent government and
fighting corruption.
The government has adopted anticorruption initiatives, says the World Bank's Mr. Mohammed, but they rarely
have led to a decrease in illegal practices. "The government has been successful in approving measures
requested by international institutions," he says. "But it frequently has a problem with their implementation."
For example, even though the government purportedly guarantees full property rights to investors, the rights
frequently aren't upheld, according to Mr. Mohammed. "If [investors] can't be sure their factory or offices will
belong to them from one day to the next, then many investors simply won't come here," he says.
As part of his government's long-term goal of joining the European Union, Prime Minister Fatos Nano has
singled out fighting corruption as a high priority ahead of elections this June. Albanians are skeptical about
such pledges because they have been made before. "This is part and parcel of every political campaign
here," says Remzi Lani, executive director of the Albanian Media Institute, a nongovernment organization that
provides support for Albanian journalists. "But there have been few tangible steps taken to tackle the
problem."
Back in his kabob shop, Kel isn't convinced it is time to leave the underground economy. He opened his shop
three years ago and says he earns enough to afford knock-off designer clothes and enjoy nights out in the
trendy bars that dot Tirana's city center.
"It's not that I don't trust the banks, but they would want records of everything I have and make," he says. "If
the government doesn't punish those who start to pay taxes and the banks make it easy to borrow money,
maybe. But I'm not in any hurry."

Shanghai Stands to Gain Clout --- China's Central Bank Mulls Moving Activities to Financial Hub
World Stock Markets
Shanghai Stands to Gain Clout --- China's Central Bank Mulls Moving Activities to Financial Hub
By J.R. Wu Dow Jones Newswires
819 words
16 March 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Shanghai -- LATE CHINESE LEADER Deng Xiaoping's long-held vision of again making Shanghai a world
financial center is gradually taking shape.
The city is home to more foreign banks than any other city in China. It hosts the nation's stock market and
exchanges for trading gold, copper, aluminum, rubber and fuel oil. Interbank funding and foreign-exchange
trading are also centered here.
Now the nation's central bank is expected to relocate a number of divisions and bureaus from its
headquarters in Beijing to Shanghai, although Beijing has so far been vague about its plans.
The governor of the central bank, Zhou Xiaochuan, said this month that clearing of central-bank
transactions and credit systems will be moved to Shanghai, state media reported.
However, a vice governor subsequently said a study is yet to begin on a possible division of work. A
spokesman for the central bank declined to comment further.
Local media speculate that the central bank will develop dual headquarters. But the division between Beijing
and Shanghai could be more like the New York Federal Reserve's relationship to the Fed in Washington.
Although the pulse of U.S. financial markets beats stronger in New York City, giving New York's Fed a higher
profile among its Fed brethren, the U.S. capital remains the apex of Fed hierarchy.
Still, even a second central-bank headquarters in Shanghai would be welcome as any face-to-face contact
with regulators and policy makers is helpful, said Fu Qing, assistant vice president at ABN Amro Bank in
Shanghai. "I want to understand as early as possible what [policy makers] are thinking," he said.
Financial-market participants want a central-bank office in Shanghai that has sufficient power to approve or
discuss financial-sector matters that might arise in their day-to-day operations, such as the central bank's
weekly open-market operations that even out the flow of liquidity through the country's banking system.
Market participants also hope that an office in the financial capital might more strongly signal approaching
policy shifts.
"The central bank sits in Beijing, far from Shanghai. Its sense and speed of reaction to us isn't ideal," said
Zhu Delin, secretary general of the Shanghai Banking Association. Mr. Zhu expects the central bank's
operations to prevent money laundering to be among six or seven units that eventually shift to Shanghai.
But the China Banking Regulatory Commission and the State Administration of Foreign Exchange, which are
offshoots of the central bank, are likely to remain in Beijing where government policy and Communist Party
decisions and approvals are made.
Some market veterans say it would be better if these regulators, along with the China Securities Regulatory
Commission, shifted to Shanghai rather than the central bank, which sets monetary policy.
Currently, the central bank's office in Shanghai is one of nine supraregional branches and covers Shanghai
municipality and neighboring Zhejiang and Fujian provinces.
