Dollar Selloff Sends Shocks Through Markets --- Stocks Plunge as Gold, Oil Surge Amid Concerns On Shift in Foreign Reserves
Dollar Selloff Sends Shocks Through Markets --- Stocks Plunge as Gold, Oil Surge Amid Concerns On
Shift in Foreign Reserves
By Craig Karmin
1,495 words
23 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A selloff of the dollar yesterday, fueled by persistent concerns over the currency's weakness, roiled financial
markets world-wide.
As the dollar registered its worst daily decline in two months, 1.4% against both the euro and the yen, stock
markets in New York, London, Paris and Frankfurt all retreated. Gold and oil prices, which tend to move
upward when the dollar is weak, rose sharply.
The selloff stemmed from news stories in South Korea that the nation's central bank, which has the world's
fourth-largest foreign reserves, at about $200 billion, was planning to diversify its foreign-exchange reserves.
"Diversification" has become a buzz word that the currency and stock traders tend to read as "dumping
dollars" -- meaning that the Korean central bank would slow its purchases of dollar-denominated securities in
favor of higher-yielding assets and other currencies, including the euro. The news from Seoul sparked fears
that other Asian central banks could follow suit.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average slipped 1.6%, the worst percentage drop in five months. The 174.02-point
loss was the index's worst decline in points since May 2003. Gold prices surged $7.40 to $434.50 and oil
prices marched back above $51 to $51.15, their highest levels since October.
The U.K.'s FTSE-100 index fell 0.6% for its biggest drop in six weeks, while benchmark indexes in Frankfurt
and Paris fell by a similar percentage.
The reaction provided a keen reminder of how quickly jitters over the fragile dollar can escalate, touching
every corner of financial markets. The chief worry is that a weak dollar could stoke inflation by raising the
cost of imported goods, and that in turn could lead to rising interest rates and slowing economic growth.
And yet there is little hard evidence that any of the world's major central banks plan a significant
diversification out of dollars anytime soon. Indeed, early Wednesday, local time, in Asia, South Korean and
Japanese government officials denied any plans to sell dollars to diversify reserves.
But 24 hours earlier, it was a different story moving the markets. With the initial news from Korea, currency
traders pushed the dollar markedly lower. Oil and gold began to rise briskly. The stock market initially
disregarded the moves, but with oil heading above $50 once again, concerns about energy costs damping
economic growth started to penetrate. By midday, stock prices began retreating more sharply, with major
market measures ending the session near or at their lows. Declining stocks outpaced gainers on the New
York Stock Exchange and Nasdaq Stock Market by more than a 2-to-1 margin, and volume on the Big Board
was the highest this year.
"There's no question that the dollar's weakness underpinned all these big swings in global markets today,"
said Jack Ablin, chief investment officer for Harris Trust &Savings Bank in Chicago.
Concern over yawning U.S. budget and trade deficits has been the driving force behind three years of dollar
declines. Those declines had halted and at least temporarily reversed a bit in the first seven weeks of the
year. But the concern has remained as progress in reining in both deficits has been fitful.
Although foreign central banks have held relatively steady in recent years with an average of more than 60%
of their foreign reserves in dollars, many economists believe that diversification by these banks into the euro
and other currencies is inevitable over time, and that the process could be accelerated if the dollar is seen to
be mired in a long-term bear market.
Yet some analysts argue that this shift will be a very gradual one, and that the Bank of Korea and other large
central banks won't be making any dramatic changes. Bank of Korea governor Park Seung recently told the
Central Banking journal: "Although the central bank is monitoring the trend of the weakening dollar and its

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
effect on the international financial market quite carefully, it has no plans to change the dollar portion of the
reserves significantly at this time."
Moreover, Japan and China -- the two largest holders of foreign reserves -- don't appear to be losing their
appetite for dollars just yet. As this becomes clearer to the market, some expect the dollar may recoup part of
its recent losses. China has more than $600 billion in currency reserves and Japan more than $800 billion.
"China and Japan are the ones to pay attention to," says Rebecca McCaughrin, a Morgan Stanley economist
in London. "We see no evidence that they are diversifying."
Since last year, ministers from members of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries have made it
clear that they are sensitive to the impact of the dollar's declining value. Because oil is priced in the dollars,
the oil producers' purchasing power world-wide is hurt by a falling dollar. They frequently cite a weaker dollar
as a justification for higher oil prices.
The 5.8% jump in oil prices yesterday contributed to the market's gloom, particularly since it closed above the
psychologically important $50 level. The jump in crude oil follows a higher-than-expected gain in the core
index of the producer-price report last week. Economists worry that continued high oil prices could be a
problem for the U.S. economy and stock market because higher energy costs might cut into corporate profits
and harm consumer sentiment.
A falling dollar isn't necessarily bad for U.S. stocks, since a weaker currency makes U.S. exports more
globally competitive and boosts foreign subsidiary earnings when translated back to dollars. But some traders
noted that if the dollar falls too quickly, it would be bearish for U.S. stocks, and even a continued gradual
decline makes U.S. stocks and bonds less attractive to foreign investors.
"The thought process over there is that the dollar will continue to weaken," says John O'Donoghue, a stock
trader for Credit Suisse First Boston, referring to European-based investors. "They see no reason to buy an
asset that will lose value for them on foreign exchange."
The dollar's losses were so steep yesterday that the euro was able to reclaim -- in one trading day -- about
40% of the ground it had given up to the dollar over the first seven weeks of this year. The decline highlighted
how even after three years of a weakening dollar, most traders remain bearish on the currency and that
short-term gains, such as those registered at the beginning of this year, are likely to remain fragile.
While central banks in Russia and the Middle East have also indicated their intentions to diversify reserve
holdings, South Korea's announcement was seen as raising the stakes. In fact, the bank early Wednesday
said it will maintain the same proportion of its dollars in reserves going forward. The diversification is out of
treasuries in other currencies and into other kinds of assets, like mortgage-backed securities.
The currency market has been especially attuned to any central-bank announcement that it is altering its
foreign-reserve holdings. "People are terrified of central-bank diversification," says Carl Weinberg, chief
economist for High Frequency Economics in Valhalla, N.Y.
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan is among those who think the issue has been overplayed in
recent weeks. Testifying before Congress last week, he said that in the case of foreign central banks, "the
extent of holdings remains very heavy for dollars." He suggested that a decline in the value of dollars in some
central-bank portfolios reflected a fall in the dollar's value versus the European currency, not a sale of
dollars.
Even so, central banks' foreign-currency reserves promise to be an important factor in the fate of the dollar for
years to come. Central banks use their reserves as a way of protecting their currencies against speculators
and to ensure that their local banks have sufficient funds to meet any demands.
In a recent report, Morgan Stanley argues that despite recent speculation and anecdotal evidence that central
banks are diversifying, the broad numbers, so far at least, don't back it up. The most comprehensive figures
on central-bank holdings for 2004 will come from the International Monetary Fund, which will release a report
in early 2006, and from the Bank for International Settlements.
But Morgan Stanley looked at recent data from the Federal Reserve, the U.S. Treasury and the Bureau of
Economic Affairs on foreign holdings of U.S. assets to get a snapshot of what the data will show. "The market
will be surprised by the relatively small diversification into euro assets in 2004," the report concludes.
"Diversification by [central banks] is a long-term objective: it will not occur when the U.S. dollar is under
pressure."

Dow Industrials Fall 174 Points With the Dollar
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Fall 174 Points With the Dollar
By Peter A. McKay
465 words
23 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE RISE IN OIL prices and a flagging dollar broadly pummeled stocks. The Dow Jones Industrial Average
saw its largest point drop since May 2003 and the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index its biggest point decline
in six months.
The Dow dropped 174.02 points, or 1.6%, to 10611.20. Of the 30 Dow stocks, only Verizon Communications
Inc. rose, scratching out a seven-cent rise to $35.38 on the New York Stock Exchange; that rescued the
blue-chip index from its first across-the-board decline in five months.
The S&P 500 fell 1.5%, or 17.43 points, to 1184.16. And the Nasdaq Composite Index lost 1.4%, or 28.30
points, to finish at 2030.32. The dollar tumbled.
Crude-oil futures leapt 5.8%, or $2.80, to $51.15 a barrel, the highest settlement in nearly four months at the
New York Mercantile Exchange, helped by cold snaps in the Northeastern U.S. and Europe and abetted by
the dollar, whose weakness aids commodities that are traded in dollar terms. Oil is up almost 18% this year,
although yesterday's move represented crude's first close over $50 in 2005.
Francis Gannon, senior portfolio manager at SunAmerica Mutual Funds, said inflation fears are rising, driven
by seemingly daily doses of bad news about oil, the dollar, corporate earnings, or some combination of all
three. "We're in an environment of skepticism about growth in the market," Mr. Gannon said. "I think all these
things will work themselves out, but for now, they're keeping a lot of investors away."
He added: "I'm in the camp that [the recent crude rally] is temporary, but until people actually see lower prices
materialize, they're going to be nervous."
Gold futures, which often move in the opposite direction of the dollar because the metal is considered an
alternative store of value, jumped 1.7%, or $7.40, to a 2005 high of $434.50 a troy ounce.
With this morning's scheduled release of consumer-price index data by the Commerce Department, markets
will receive a key sign whether fresh inflation fears are indeed warranted. Most economists expect a 0.2%
rise in January CPI.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks fell. On the Big Board, where 1.7 billion shares traded, 2,618 stocks fell and 732 rose.
Bonds fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 4/32, or $1.25 per $1,000 invested, pushing the yield up to 4.283%.
The 30-year bond fell 14/32 to yield 4.682%.
The dollar fell to 104.06 yen from 105.57 yen. The euro strengthened to $1.3256 from $1.3065.

Crude Rises to $51.15 a Barrel On Weather, Supply Concerns --- Longer-Term Oil Output Begins to Worry Some; CRB at 20-Year High
Commodities Report
Crude Rises to $51.15 a Barrel On Weather, Supply Concerns --- Longer-Term Oil Output Begins to
Worry Some; CRB at 20-Year High
By Leah McGrath Goodman Dow Jones Newswires
557 words
23 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude-oil futures in New York rose $2.80 a barrel to more than a three-month high, reflecting colder weather
and growing supply concerns.
Benchmark light, sweet crude futures for March delivery at the New York Mercantile Exchange settled at
$51.15 a barrel, the highest settlement price this year. The March contract expired yesterday.
Crude's higher-volume April, May, June and July contracts also ended above $51 a barrel.
The rally came alongside a sharp drop in the U.S. dollar and declines on the stock market.
While market participants are less jittery about the possibility of a production cut in March by the Organization
of Petroleum Exporting Countries, they have started to worry about longer-term oil supply, said Michael
Guido, director of commodity strategy in New York for French bank Societe Generale.
"Spare production capacity has always been an issue, but if these global oil-demand forecasts are on the
money, can supply keep up?" he asked. "OPEC seems to be at max capacity right now."
The president of OPEC yesterday assuaged worries that the group may decide to slash oil production for a
second time this year.
"The price is very high, and we have to respect that price and cooperate with others for oil-market stability,"
said Sheikh Ahmad Fahad Al-Ahmad Al-Sabah. Monday, Saudi Arabian Oil Minister Ali Naimi said
commercial oil inventories in industrial countries were at appropriate levels. OPEC, which provides more than
a third of the world's oil, plans to review its production policy March 16 in Iran.
Looking back on how the West has interacted with the Middle East, President Bush yesterday suggested that
countries could no longer accept "tyranny" in the Middle East in exchange for "cheap oil." Some traders said
the comments, made at a conference in Brussels, fanned more fears of tensions between the U.S. and
Middle East. Colder-than-normal temperatures in Europe and frosty weather in the Northeast U.S. also
supported higher prices.
The selloff in the U.S. dollar stemmed from reports Monday that the South Korean Central Bank would seek
to diversify reserves away from the dollar in search of higher yields. U.S. markets were closed Monday for the
Presidents Day holiday.
The CRB Futures Index rose to a 20-year high with the rally in crude oil, grains and metals, supported by the
dollar's weakness.
In other commodities markets:
SOYBEANS: Prices of soybean futures on the Chicago Board of Trade rallied to five-month highs as
concerns about dry conditions in southern Brazil stirred talk of smaller production. The active May contract
rose 27.25 cents to $5.83 a bushel, while the front-month March contract rose 24.50 cents to $5.77 a bushel.
GOLD: Futures on the Comex division of the Nymex rallied to their highest levels in nearly two months on
heavy speculative buying, spurred by the U.S. dollar's sharp decline and the breaching of overhead chart
resistance. The most-active April contract settled up $7.40 at $435.80 a troy ounce. The front-month February
contract rose $7.40 to $434.50.

Dollar Plunges on Worries About South Korean Plans
Currency Trading
Dollar Plunges on Worries About South Korean Plans
By Steven Vames Dow Jones Newswires
611 words
23 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar plunged, posting its largest one-day loss versus the euro since August and
dropping to a seven-year low versus the South Korean won, as reports of diversification of official reserves
again spooked the market.
Plans for South Korea's central bank to diversify its $200 billion in foreign-exchange holdings, mentioned in a
briefing from the Bank of Korea to lawmakers, didn't necessarily come as a big surprise. The statement
reflected official comments dating back more than a year that the bank was lowering the proportion of U.S.
Treasurys in its reserve holdings.
But the publicity garnered by the Bank of Korea document on Monday and yesterday helped revive one of the
looming concerns that pressure the dollar: that global central banks, discouraged by low yields and protracted
declines in the value of the dollar, could start to reduce their adherence on the dollar as a reserve currency.
That, in turn, plays into fears that the U.S. will struggle to finance its huge current-account deficit.
"The big risk from this situation is that other Far Eastern central banks begin to do the same. Remember,
Russia already has announced that they may begin increasing the size of euros in their reserves back on
November 23rd and the buck tanked on that day, too," said Andy Busch, global foreign-exchange strategist at
Harris Nesbitt in Chicago in an e-mail note yesterday.
U.S. banks had been closed Monday for the Presidents Day holiday.
In late New York trading, the euro was at $1.3256, up from $1.3065 late Monday in Toronto. The dollar was at
104.06 yen, down from 105.57 yen, and at 1.1585 Swiss francs, down from 1.1823 francs. The pound rose to
$1.9109 from $1.8968.
The dollar's drop lifted the euro more than 1.4% to its strongest level in six weeks. Meanwhile, the U.S.
currency also lost 1.4% versus the yen during yesterday's session. The dollar also lost about 2% versus the
Korean won, which traded at its highest level in more than seven years.
Spurred by the won's gains, the Taiwan dollar climbed to a four-year high against its U.S. counterpart. Other
East Asian currencies such as the Singapore dollar and the baht rose, but to a lesser degree.
A sharp rally in oil and commodity prices also put pressure on the dollar. Crude oil for March delivery ended
up $2.80 at $51.15 a barrel, its highest level since November. The Dow Jones Industrial Average sank
sharply, shedding 174.02 points to 10611.20, while the Nasdaq Composite Index dropped 28.30 points to
2030.32.
Meanwhile, gold traded up 1.7%, or $7.40 an ounce, to $434.50 an ounce, its highest level since Jan. 3. The
CRB commodity index, a broad measure of commodity futures prices, ended up $7.05 at $297.66, its highest
level since early 1981.
The strong rally in commodities, coupled with the news from South Korea, helped pushed currencies strongly
tied with commodity prices particularly higher. The U.S. dollar was at a 20-year low versus the New Zealand
dollar, and the Australian dollar was trading just below an eight-year high versus the U.S. dollar.
As currency trading resumed in Asia early Wednesday, the dollar was strengthening against its major
counterparts and against the won after South Korean and Japanese government officials denied having any
plans to sell dollars to diversify reserves.
---
Laurence Norman contributed to this article.

both generic-drug makers. The Swiss firm also will launch a nearly $1 billion tender offer to buy the remaining
31.9% of Eon that is publicly traded. The deal will transform Novartis into the top seller of generic drugs.

Dollar Falls Again Versus Euro Despite Firm Economic Data --- U.S. Currency Shrugs Off Comments From Fed Chief; Recent Rally Looks Remote
Currency Trading
Dollar Falls Again Versus Euro Despite Firm Economic Data --- U.S. Currency Shrugs Off Comments
From Fed Chief; Recent Rally Looks Remote
By Laurence Norman Dow Jones Newswires
543 words
18 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar fell versus the euro for the seventh straight session, losing ground despite generally firm U.S.
economic data.
The dollar also largely brushed off the second day of testimony from Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan in Congress. Appearing before the House Financial Services Committee, Mr. Greenspan largely
reiterated the views he had expressed in the Senate Wednesday.
With the dollar continuing to decline despite the strong data and the generally upbeat economic picture Mr.
Greenspan painted, analysts said it was increasingly clear that the dollar was unlikely to return to the levels it
rallied to in early February.
That was underlined by a broad rise in base metal prices, as hedge funds took money out of the
foreign-exchange markets and placed them in gold, copper and other metals. April gold contracts closed at a
three-week high, up $1.70 at $428.60.
In late New York trading, the euro was at $1.3078, compared with $1.3027 late Wednesday. The dollar was
at 105.54 yen, up slightly from 105.44 yen and at 1.1832 Swiss francs, from 1.1876 francs. Sterling lifted to
$1.8955 from $1.8849.
The euro was at a six-week high versus the Japanese currency at 137.97 yen, from 137.40 yen Wednesday.
The dollar was little changed in early New York trading, after recovering overnight losses when January U.S.
import prices rose 0.9% last month versus a 0.5% consensus. A fall in weekly jobless claims to their lowest
level since 2000 also beat market expectations and briefly lifted the dollar.
However, the U.S. currency quickly resumed its declines, as investors continued the dollar selling that has
characterized the last week of session.
The euro reached a session high of $1.3089 after January leading indicators fell more than expected and it
stayed around those levels despite a rise in the Philadelphia Federal Reserve Bank business conditions
index, which rose to 23.9 for February versus a forecast of 17.8 and from 13.2 in January.
Analysts said the dollar was also sold after currency traders saw the U.S. currency wasn't getting much of a
lift from higher Treasury yields. Ten-year U.S. Treasury yields spiked Wednesday after Mr. Greenspan said
the low interest rates on longer dated notes were a "conundrum." The yield on 10-year notes rose again to
4.189%.
Traders said the euro's next target was the $1.31 mark. Options barriers stopped the dollar falling below that
level, but they may not prove protection enough today, if the U.S. January producer-price index surprises on
the upside. The consensus is for a 0.3% rise in wholesale prices.
The exception to the dollar weakness story was the yen. The Japanese currency has lagged behind the
euro's recent rise versus the dollar because of another bout of poor economic data. On Wednesday, Japan
reported a third successive quarter of economic contraction. Overnight, Japanese officials made it clear they
wouldn't loosen monetary policy in the current economic context.

Review / Books: On the Trail of Hollywood Gold
Review / Books:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Review / Books: On the Trail of Hollywood Gold
By Jonathan V. Last
1,300 words
18 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W6
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE BIG PICTURE
By Edward Jay Epstein
(Random House, 375 pages, $24.95)
A SURVEY of the muck soon to be celebrated at the Academy Awards confirms William Goldman's sad
truism: Every Oscar night you look back and realize that last year was the worst year in the history of
Hollywood.
It is depressing to watch the quality of American cinema degrade, as if it were some kind of glittery
radioactive isotope. But Edward Jay Epstein provides comfort with "The Big Picture," offering a compelling
backstory to the awfulness we see at the cineplex.
"The Big Picture" is Hollywood's "Moneyball" -- a groundbreaking work that explains the inner workings of the
game. (Movies in this case, not baseball, although each could probably learn from the other.) There is, Mr.
Epstein argues, a powerful economic reason that movies aren't very good anymore: They don't have to be.
"The Big Picture" begins with the birth of the studio system at the turn of the 20th century, a world of
privileges and profit sources that contrasts strikingly with the current maze of studios, entertainment
conglomerates and media barons. It is difficult to imagine now, but Hollywood was once run by a mere
handful of men.
They had writers, actors and directors under contract, to whom they assigned projects based on their own
whims. They churned out a fantastic number of movies -- cheaply -- and exhibited them in theaters they
owned. And the public couldn't get enough. In 1947, Hollywood sold 4.7 billion movie tickets. The studios
were hugely profitable movie factories.
Times have changed. In 1949, invoking the Sherman Anti-Trust Act, the Justice Department forced the
studios to divest themselves of their theaters. Over the years actors, directors and other artisans redefined
themselves as free agents. Television came to compete with the movies, as did home video. And despite a
population boom, movie-going fell out of favor. In 2003, only 1.57 billion tickets were sold, a third the number
56 years earlier, while the real cost of making movies increased some 1,600%.
It wasn't just production costs that exploded. Today the average movie costs $4.2 million to distribute and
nearly $35 million just to advertise. (The comparable 1947 figures, adjusted for inflation, were $550,000 and
$300,000.) Such peripheral costs, Mr. Epstein explains, have grown so large that "even if the studios had
somehow managed to obtain all their movies for free, they would still have lost money on their American
releases."
What happened? Hollywood redefined itself too, Mr. Epstein argues -- as a clearinghouse for intellectual
property, not a factory for making movies. This new business is at least as profitable as the old one, but the
"product" on offer is different. To explain, follow Mr. Epstein's detailed analysis of the movie "Gone in 60
Seconds."
In 2000, "Gone," an action-thriller, was released to little acclaim and somewhat disappointing box-office
returns. The company that produced it, Touchstone, was (and is) part of the Disney empire. That year, Disney
touted the global box-office revenue of "Gone" as $242 million. Not bad. Even if theaters kept $139.8 million
from ticket sales, Disney still took in $102.2 million. Surely there was a profit in there somewhere?

--- Page 211 ---
Page 211 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Not necessarily. Consider the expenses. The physical production of the movie was $103.3 million. Prints cost
$13 million; insurance, taxes and customs clearance came to almost as much. The studio spent $42 million
for advertising in North America and a bit more than half of that for the rest of the globe. On the back end,
Disney paid out $12.6 million in residual fees and figured in $17.2 million for overhead and $41.8 million for
debt service -- for a total negative cost of $265.3 million, more than double the studio's take of the box-office
receipts.
So how did Disney make money? The answer is in the clearinghouse. Disney never expected to profit from
the theatrical release of "Gone in 60 Seconds," but it did count on harnessing a whole river of money -- from
the rights to the intellectual property it had created.
By 2002, Buena Vista Home Entertainment International, another division of Disney, had reaped $198 million
in sales and rentals from "Gone in 60 Seconds" videos and DVDs. Only $19 million of that sum was credited
to the movie itself, though, thanks to the complicated royalty system that Hollywood employs. This reduced
number is an important accounting trick since the movie's star, Nicholas Cage, was contractually entitled to
10% of the video gross.
Indeed, one of the key components of the clearinghouse system -- boosting studio revenue enormously -- is
hiding income from a movie's (seeming) profit-participants. There is nothing illegal about it, although the effect
is a nasty little game of hide and seek. One of the virtues of "The Big Picture" is Mr. Epstein's astonishing
access to numbers that the movie studios go to great lengths to keep secret, so as not to offend people like
Mr. Cage.
In coming years, Disney can expect a steady, if diminished, stream of income from these "Gone" home-video
sales. But there is more. HBO paid Disney $18.2 million for the rights to air the movie (of which only $2.7
million was exposed to parties entitled to residuals). Once HBO's deal expired, it migrated onto cable's TNT
network for another payout. Disney will continue to collect money from "Gone" whenever domestic cable or
network television shows it. In a few years, local TV stations will fork over to Disney still more millions when
their window finally opens on purchase rights. Still later there will be cash from foreign TV markets. And let's
not forget income from product licensing and soundtrack sales.
The truth is that, even with terrible movies, the studios have to try hard not to make money. In this way,
today's Hollywood is very much like the studio system of old. The two business models are so favorable that
the quality of the product is beside the point. The difference, of course, is that the movies from the studio era
were often quite good.
If "The Big Picture" has a flaw, it is that Mr. Epstein does not explore the question of why one era produced
better movies than the other. Part of the answer revolves around the popcorn economy that Mr. Epstein
details so authoritatively. Once the studios divested themselves of their theaters, theater-owners needed a
way to make their businesses solvent, and concessions were the solution. Today popcorn sales keep movie
theaters afloat. Adolescents are the primary popcorn consumers and the group most likely to see a film in its
first week of release. These two facts create pressure for teen-friendly films with intentionally short shelf-lives.
A recipe for junk.
Occasionally Mr. Epstein gets a detail wrong. He reports, for instance, that the term "blockbuster" comes from
lines forming around the block when in fact Variety coined it in 1951, likening the box-office success of "Quo
Vadis?" to a World War II aerial bomb. Elsewhere he overestimates the cost of making theatrical trailers.
Some may approach a million dollars, but the average is about $300,000.
But such minor matters do not obscure Mr. Epstein's impressive achievement. He has produced an insightful
work of forensic accounting and a keen industry analysis, full of surprising numbers and vivid examples. His
candid insider sources perhaps reveal more than they intended. All the stars in the Hollywood firmament
should read "The Big Picture" before they negotiate their next contract.
---
Mr. Last is online editor of The Weekly Standard.
